Negatives of Equal Universals and the Encompassing Concepts Doubt
Author explains the rule and a doubt about empty negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where the negatives of two equal universals are not equal due to concept emptiness, and explain why the exception applies only to existential concepts.

Core Idea
Negation flips equality to equality only when negatives have instances.

The author establishes a general rule: the negatives of two equal universals are also equal. But he immediately raises a strong doubt involving all-encompassing concepts like 'thing' and 'possible'. Understanding this exception requires distinguishing between existential and negative concepts—a crucial move in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Find where the author states that the negatives of equal universals are equal. The reasoning is that if they were not equal, the equal universals would diverge, which is absurd.
The claim is from the first few lines: نقيضا المتساويين متساويان
2
Pinpoint the doubt
The author notes that the negative of co-extensiveness is merely its removal, not the truth of divergence. Moreover, the negatives of equal universals might be concepts with no individuals (e.g., negatives of all-encompassing concepts). In that case, lack of co-extensiveness can be true without divergence.
Example: the negative of 'thing' is 'non-thing', which applies to nothing.
3
Analyze the proposed solution
The author considers a response: the truth of negation does not entail existence, but the removal of co-extensiveness implies divergence only for existential concepts (like 'thing' and 'possible'). For purely negative concepts (like 'no partner of the Creator'), the implication fails.
Existential concepts have positive content; negative concepts are privative.
4
Apply the principle
The solution limits the original claim to concepts whose negatives are non-empty. For all-encompassing existential concepts, the negatives are empty, so the rule of equality does not apply.
This protects the rule from counterexamples involving impossible concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ نَقِيضَ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ رَفْعُهُ، فَنَقِيضَا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَتَفَارَقَا فِي الصِّدْقِ، فَيَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ أَحَدِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ بِدُونِ الْآخَرِ، هَذَا خُلْفٌ. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ قَوِيٌّ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ نَقِيضَ التَّصَادُقِ رَفْعُهُ، لَا صِدْقُ التَّفَارُقِ، وَرُبَّمَا يَكُونُ نَقِيضَا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مِمَّا لَا فَرْدَ لَهُ أَصْلًا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، كَنَقَائِضِ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ لِكُلِّ مَفْهُومٍ، فَيَصْدُقُ الْأَوَّلُ، أَيْ عَدَمُ التَّصَادُقِ، دُونَ الثَّانِي، أَيْ التَّفَارُقِ. وَمَا قِيلَ: إِنَّ صِدْقَ السَّلْبِ عَلَى شَيْءٍ لَا يَقْتَضِي وُجُودَهُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ رَفْعُ التَّصَادُقِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ التَّفَارُقَ، فَبَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِهِ إِنَّمَا يَتِمُّ لَوْ أُخِذَتْ تِلْكَ الْمَفْهُومَاتُ وُجُودِيَّةً، كَالشَّيْءِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ، أَمَّا إِذَا كَانَتْ سَلْبِيَّةً كَلَا شَرِيكَ الْبَارِي وَلَا اجْتِمَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ فَلَا مَسَاغَ لِذَلِكَ فِيهِ. فَلَا جَوَابَ إِلَّا بِتَخْصِيصِ الدَّعْوَى بِغَيْرِ نَقَائِضِ تِلْكَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ. هَذَا، وَنَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصِّ مُطْلَقًا بِالْعَكْسِ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامِّ مَلْزُومٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاصِّ، وَلَا عَكْسَ؛ تَحْقِيقًا لِمَعْنَى الْعُمُومِ. وَشُكِّكَ بِأَنَّ لَا اجْتِمَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ، مَعَ أَنَّ بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْهِمَا تَبَايُنًا كُلِّيًّا، وَأَيْضًا الْمُمْكِنُ الْعَامُّ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنِ الْخَاصِّ، فَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِنٍ عَامٍ لَا مُمْكِنٌ خَاصٌّ، وَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِنٍ خَاصٍّ إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ، وَكِلَاهُمَا مُمْكِنٌ عَامٌ، فَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِنٍ عَامٍ مُمْكِنٌ عَامٌ، وَالْجَوَابُ بِمَا مَرَّ. وَبَيْنَ نَقِيضَيِ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصِّ مِنْ وَجْهٍ تَبَايُنٌ جُزْئِيٌّ كَالْمُتَبَايِنَيْنِ، وَهُوَ التَّفَارُقُ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ؛ لِأَنَّ بَيْنَ الْعَيْنَيْنِ تَفَارُقًا، فَحَيْثُ يَصْدُقُ عَيْنُ أَحَدِهِمَا يَصْدُقُ نَقِيضُ الْآخَرِ، وَهُوَ قَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي ضِمْنِ التَّبَايُنِ الْكُلِّيِّ، كَاللَّا حَجَرِ
Know that the negative of everything is its removal. So the two negatives of the two equal universals are equal; otherwise they would diverge in truth, and it would follow that one of the two equal universals is true without the other — this is absurd. And here is a strong doubt: that the negative of co-extensiveness is its removal, not the truth of divergence. And perhaps the two negatives of the two equal universals are among those that have no individual at all in the realm of reality, like the negatives of concepts that encompass every concept. So the first, i.e., lack of co-extensiveness, would be true without the second, i.e., divergence. And what is said: that the truth of negation about something does not entail its existence, and then the removal of co-extensiveness entails divergence; after conceding this, it only holds if those concepts are taken as existential, like 'thing' and 'possible'. But if they are negative, like 'no partner of the Creator' and 'no conjunction of the two contradictories', then there is no room for that. So there is no answer except by limiting the claim to other than the negatives of those encompassing concepts. This, and the negative of the more general and more specific absolutely is by reversal; for the non-existence of the general entails the non-existence of the specific, and not vice versa; to realize the meaning of generality. And it was doubted that 'no conjunction of the two contradictories' is more general than 'human', even though between their two negatives there is complete disparity. And also the general possible is more general than the specific possible; so every non-general possible is a non-specific possible, and every non-specific possible is either necessary or impossible, and both are general possible, so every non-general possible is a general possible. And the answer is by what has passed. And between the two negatives of the more general and more specific in one respect there is partial disparity like the two disparate ones, and it is divergence in general; because there is divergence between them, so whenever the positive of one of them is true, the negative of the other is true, and it may occur within total disparity, like 'non-stone'.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical behavior of the negatives (naqid) of the four universal relations. The author begins with a general principle: the negative of something is its removal (raf'). For the two equal universals, their negatives are also equal; otherwise it would lead to absurdity. A strong doubt is raised: the negative of co-extensiveness is simply the removal of that co-extensiveness, not the affirmation of divergence (tafaruq). Moreover, the negatives of equal universals might be concepts that have no instances at all (e.g., contradictories of all-encompassing concepts like 'thing'). In that case, the lack of co-extensiveness can be true without divergence being true because there are no individuals to which the negatives apply. The author considers a possible response: the truth of a negation does not require the existence of the subject, and the removal of co-extensiveness implies divergence, but only for existential concepts (like 'thing' and 'possible'). For purely negative concepts (like 'no partner of God' or 'no conjunction of contradictories'), this does not hold. Therefore, the claim must be restricted to concepts other than those special negatives. Next, for the relation of absolutely general and absolutely specific, the rule is by reversal (bi'l-aks): the non-existence of the general entails the non-existence of the specific, but not vice versa. A doubt is examined using the example 'no conjunction of contradictories' (a negative concept) and 'human'. Though 'no conjunction of contradictories' is more general, their negatives are wholly disparate because one is negative and the other positive. Another doubt involves the general possible and specific possible, leading to a paradoxical implication that every non-general possible is a general possible; but the answer is the same as before, relying on the distinction between existential and negative concepts. Finally, for the relation of a'am wa akhass min wajh, their negatives have partial disparity (tabayun juz'i), similar to disparate universals. Specifically, the positive of one implies the negative of the other. This partial disparity can occur within a total disparity, as in the example of 'non-stone', which is a wide negative concept. The whole passage illustrates the subtle effects of negation on universal relations, especially when dealing with negative or all-encompassing concepts.

Key Terms
النقيض
التصادق
التفارق
السلب
الوجودية والسالبة
المفاهيم الشاملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the condition under which the negatives of two equal universals are NOT equal?
A) When the original universals are not actually equal
B) When the negatives are negative (salbi) all-encompassing concepts with no instances
C) When the negatives are existential (wujudi) concepts
D) When the original universals are disparate

Key Takeaway
The rule that negatives of equal universals are equal holds only when the negatives are non-empty; for negative all-encompassing concepts, the rule requires restriction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the equal concepts 'human' and 'rational animal'. Test whether their negatives 'non-human' and 'non-rational animal' are equal. Identify any condition that might break equality.
?
The concept 'possible' (al-mumkin) is all-encompassing in its domain. Should we expect its negative 'impossible' to behave the same as negatives of other equal concepts? Explain using the author's distinction.

Negatives of General-Specific Universals: Reversal and Partial Disparity
Author explains behavior of negatives for general-specific relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the relation between the negatives of two concepts in a general-specific absolute relationship, and distinguish it from the case of general-specific in one respect.

Core Idea
Negation reverses the direction of implication for general-specific absolute, but creates partial disparity for partial general-specific.

The author extends the analysis to the other two universal relations. For the general-specific absolute (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan), the negatives obey a reversal: the negative of the general implies the negative of the specific, but not vice versa. A doubt involving the concept 'no conjunction of contradictories' shows a counterexample when the negative of the general is an impossible concept. For the general-specific in one respect (min wajh), the negatives exhibit partial disparity, similar to disparate universals.

Guided Path
1
State the reversal rule
For concepts where one is absolutely more general than the other (e.g., animal vs. human), the negative of the general (non-animal) implies the negative of the specific (non-human). But the reverse is not true: non-human does not imply non-animal, because there are non-humans that are animals.
This is a direct logical consequence: if all B are A, then not-A implies not-B.
2
Test with the first doubt
The author presents the example: 'no conjunction of contradictories' is more general than 'human', yet their negatives ('conjunction of contradictories' and 'non-human') are fully disparate (one impossible, one possible). This violates the reversal rule because the negative of the general is impossible (empty).
The rule assumed that the negative of the general is existent, but here it's impossible.
3
Analyze the second doubt about 'possible'
The author considers 'general possible' (mumkin 'amm) and 'specific possible' (mumkin khass). The reversal rule seems to imply that every non-general possible is a non-specific possible, and non-specific possible is either necessary or impossible, both of which are general possible, leading to a paradox. The answer again relies on the distinction between existential and negative concepts.
This paradox shows the reversal rule fails for concepts that are negative or encompassing.
4
Handle partial general-specific
For concepts that are general in one respect and specific in another (e.g., 'black' and 'stone'), their negatives have partial disparity: the positive of one implies the negative of the other. For example, if x is black, then x is non-stone (since black and stone are not co-extensive), but not vice versa.
The author likens this to the relation between two disparate universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ نَقِيضَ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ رَفْعُهُ، فَنَقِيضَا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَتَفَارَقَا فِي الصِّدْقِ، فَيَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ أَحَدِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ بِدُونِ الْآخَرِ، هَذَا خُلْفٌ. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ قَوِيٌّ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ نَقِيضَ التَّصَادُقِ رَفْعُهُ، لَا صِدْقُ التَّفَارُقِ، وَرُبَّمَا يَكُونُ نَقِيضَا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مِمَّا لَا فَرْدَ لَهُ أَصْلًا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، كَنَقَائِضِ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ لِكُلِّ مَفْهُومٍ، فَيَصْدُقُ الْأَوَّلُ، أَيْ عَدَمُ التَّصَادُقِ، دُونَ الثَّانِي، أَيْ التَّفَارُقِ. وَمَا قِيلَ: إِنَّ صِدْقَ السَّلْبِ عَلَى شَيْءٍ لَا يَقْتَضِي وُجُودَهُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ رَفْعُ التَّصَادُقِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ التَّفَارُقَ، فَبَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِهِ إِنَّمَا يَتِمُّ لَوْ أُخِذَتْ تِلْكَ الْمَفْهُومَاتُ وُجُودِيَّةً، كَالشَّيْءِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ، أَمَّا إِذَا كَانَتْ سَلْبِيَّةً كَلَا شَرِيكَ الْبَارِي وَلَا اجْتِمَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ فَلَا مَسَاغَ لِذَلِكَ فِيهِ. فَلَا جَوَابَ إِلَّا بِتَخْصِيصِ الدَّعْوَى بِغَيْرِ نَقَائِضِ تِلْكَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ. هَذَا، وَنَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصِّ مُطْلَقًا بِالْعَكْسِ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامِّ مَلْزُومٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاصِّ، وَلَا عَكْسَ؛ تَحْقِيقًا لِمَعْنَى الْعُمُومِ. وَشُكِّكَ بِأَنَّ لَا اجْتِمَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ، مَعَ أَنَّ بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْهِمَا تَبَايُنًا كُلِّيًّا، وَأَيْضًا الْمُمْكِنُ الْعَامُّ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنِ الْخَاصِّ، فَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِنٍ عَامٍ لَا مُمْكِنٌ خَاصٌّ، وَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِنٍ خَاصٍّ إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ، وَكِلَاهُمَا مُمْكِنٌ عَامٌ، فَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِنٍ عَامٍ مُمْكِنٌ عَامٌ، وَالْجَوَابُ بِمَا مَرَّ. وَبَيْنَ نَقِيضَيِ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصِّ مِنْ وَجْهٍ تَبَايُنٌ جُزْئِيٌّ كَالْمُتَبَايِنَيْنِ، وَهُوَ التَّفَارُقُ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ؛ لِأَنَّ بَيْنَ الْعَيْنَيْنِ تَفَارُقًا، فَحَيْثُ يَصْدُقُ عَيْنُ أَحَدِهِمَا يَصْدُقُ نَقِيضُ الْآخَرِ، وَهُوَ قَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي ضِمْنِ التَّبَايُنِ الْكُلِّيِّ، كَاللَّا حَجَرِ
Know that the negative of everything is its removal. So the two negatives of the two equal universals are equal; otherwise they would diverge in truth, and it would follow that one of the two equal universals is true without the other — this is absurd. And here is a strong doubt: that the negative of co-extensiveness is its removal, not the truth of divergence. And perhaps the two negatives of the two equal universals are among those that have no individual at all in the realm of reality, like the negatives of concepts that encompass every concept. So the first, i.e., lack of co-extensiveness, would be true without the second, i.e., divergence. And what is said: that the truth of negation about something does not entail its existence, and then the removal of co-extensiveness entails divergence; after conceding this, it only holds if those concepts are taken as existential, like 'thing' and 'possible'. But if they are negative, like 'no partner of the Creator' and 'no conjunction of the two contradictories', then there is no room for that. So there is no answer except by limiting the claim to other than the negatives of those encompassing concepts. This, and the negative of the more general and more specific absolutely is by reversal; for the non-existence of the general entails the non-existence of the specific, and not vice versa; to realize the meaning of generality. And it was doubted that 'no conjunction of the two contradictories' is more general than 'human', even though between their two negatives there is complete disparity. And also the general possible is more general than the specific possible; so every non-general possible is a non-specific possible, and every non-specific possible is either necessary or impossible, and both are general possible, so every non-general possible is a general possible. And the answer is by what has passed. And between the two negatives of the more general and more specific in one respect there is partial disparity like the two disparate ones, and it is divergence in general; because there is divergence between them, so whenever the positive of one of them is true, the negative of the other is true, and it may occur within total disparity, like 'non-stone'.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical behavior of the negatives (naqid) of the four universal relations. The author begins with a general principle: the negative of something is its removal (raf'). For the two equal universals, their negatives are also equal; otherwise it would lead to absurdity. A strong doubt is raised: the negative of co-extensiveness is simply the removal of that co-extensiveness, not the affirmation of divergence (tafaruq). Moreover, the negatives of equal universals might be concepts that have no instances at all (e.g., contradictories of all-encompassing concepts like 'thing'). In that case, the lack of co-extensiveness can be true without divergence being true because there are no individuals to which the negatives apply. The author considers a possible response: the truth of a negation does not require the existence of the subject, and the removal of co-extensiveness implies divergence, but only for existential concepts (like 'thing' and 'possible'). For purely negative concepts (like 'no partner of God' or 'no conjunction of contradictories'), this does not hold. Therefore, the claim must be restricted to concepts other than those special negatives. Next, for the relation of absolutely general and absolutely specific, the rule is by reversal (bi'l-aks): the non-existence of the general entails the non-existence of the specific, but not vice versa. A doubt is examined using the example 'no conjunction of contradictories' (a negative concept) and 'human'. Though 'no conjunction of contradictories' is more general, their negatives are wholly disparate because one is negative and the other positive. Another doubt involves the general possible and specific possible, leading to a paradoxical implication that every non-general possible is a general possible; but the answer is the same as before, relying on the distinction between existential and negative concepts. Finally, for the relation of a'am wa akhass min wajh, their negatives have partial disparity (tabayun juz'i), similar to disparate universals. Specifically, the positive of one implies the negative of the other. This partial disparity can occur within a total disparity, as in the example of 'non-stone', which is a wide negative concept. The whole passage illustrates the subtle effects of negation on universal relations, especially when dealing with negative or all-encompassing concepts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between the negatives of two concepts that are related as a'am wa akhass mutlaqan?
A) They are equal
B) They are by reversal (the negative of the general implies the negative of the specific, but not vice versa)
C) They are fully disparate
D) They are partially overlapping

Key Takeaway
For general-specific absolute, the negatives follow the contrapositive; for general-specific in one respect, the negatives have partial disparity; both rules are subject to exceptions for negative or empty concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the general rule 'every intoxicant is haram' and its specific case 'wine'. Determine the relation between the negatives 'non-intoxicant' and 'non-haram'. Is it by reversal? Check if any condition might break it.
?
Give an example from fiqh where a partial general-specific relation exists between two concepts (e.g., 'sale' and 'contract'). What does the author's rule say about their negatives?

The Contradictories of Equal Terms and a Strong Objection
Author raises an objection about the contradiction of equal terms when the contradictory has no instance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the claim 'contradictories of equal terms are equal' holds and when it fails due to concepts with no instances in reality.

Core Idea
Contradictories of equal terms are not always equal when one contradictory has no real instance.

The author begins by defining contradictory as removal and arguing that if two terms are equal (co-extensive), their contradictories must also be equal. But then he poses a strong objection: what if the contradictory of a concept has no instances at all, like the contradictory of a universal negative concept? This forces us to restrict the original claim. In this lesson, we will walk through the reasoning and learn how to handle such edge cases, which are crucial in Islamic legal theory when dealing with categories like wajib and haram.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of contradictory
The author says: 'the contradictory of each thing is its removal (رفع).' This means the contradictory of a term or proposition is simply its negation. Grasp this basic definition before proceeding.
2
Follow the proof that contradictories of equal terms are equal
Assume two terms A and B are equal (they apply to exactly the same things). Suppose their contradictories, not-A and not-B, are not equal: then there is some subject of which one is true and the other false. That would imply that the original terms differ in truth value on that subject, contradicting their equality. So the contradictories must be equal.
This is a simple reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the objection: the case of concepts with no instances
The author points out a problem: the contradictory of 'mutual truth' (tasāduq) is simply its removal, not 'difference' (tafāruq). Moreover, sometimes the contradictory of a term may have no individual at all in reality, like the contradictory of a universal concept that covers everything. For example, the contradictory of 'thing' is 'non-thing', which has no instances. In such cases, the absence of mutual truth (i.e., that two terms never apply together) can be true even if the contradictories are not equal.
This objection challenges the universal applicability of the earlier claim.
4
Analyze the proposed response and the restriction
One might say that a negative proposition can be true without the subject existing, so the removal of mutual truth entails difference. But the author counters that this only works if the concepts are existential (like 'thing' or 'possible'). For negative concepts like 'not a partner of the Creator' (lā sharīka al-bārī), the contradictory 'partner of the Creator' has no instance, and the response fails. Therefore, the original claim must be restricted to concepts that are affirmative and have positive instances.
This shows the importance of considering the nature of the terms involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ نَقِيضَ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ رَفْعُهُ ، فَنَقِيضًا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَتَفَارَقَا في الصِّدْقِ ، فَيَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ أَحَدِ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ بِدُونِ الْآخَرِ، هَذَا خُلْفٌ. وَهَهُنَا شَلٌّ قَوِيٌّ ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ نَقِيض التَّصَادُقِ رَفْعُهُ، لَا صِدْقُ التَّفَارُقِ، وَرُبَّمَا يَكُونُ نَقِيضُ الْمُتَسَاوِبَيْنِ مِمَّا لَا فَرْدَ لَهُ أَصْلاً فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، كَنَفَائِضِ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ لِكُلِّ مَفْهُومٍ ، فَيَصْدُقُ الْأَوَّلُ، أَيْ عَدَمُ التَّصَادِقِ، دُونَ الثَّانِي ، أَيْ التَّفَارُقِ. وَمَا قِيلَ : إِنَّ صِدْقَ السَّلْبِ عَلَى شَيْءٍ لَا يَقْتَضِي وُجُودَهُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ رَفْعُ التَّصَادُقِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ التَّفَارُقَ ، فَبَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِهِ إِنَّمَا يَتِمُّ لَوْ أُخِذَتْ تِلْكَ الْمَفْهُومَاتُ وُجُودِيَّةٌ ، كَالشَّيْءِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ ، أَمَّا إِذَا كَانَتْ سَلْبِيَّةٌ كَلَاشَرِيكِ الْبَارِي وَلَا اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ فَلَا مَسَاغَ لِذَلِكَ فِيهِ. فَلَا جَوَابَ إِلَّا بِتَخْصِيصِ الدَّعْوَى بِغَيْرِ نَفَائِضِ تِلْكَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ.
And know that the contradictory of each thing is its removal. So the contradictories of two equal things are equal; otherwise they would differ in truth value, for then it would be necessary that one of the two equals is true without the other — this is absurd. And here there is a strong objection: that the contradictory of mutual truth (tasāduq) is its removal, not the truth of difference. And sometimes the contradictory of two equals is that which has no individual at all in reality, like the contradictories of concepts that encompass every concept. Then the first would be true, i.e., absence of mutual truth, without the second, i.e., difference. And what was said — that the truth of a negative about something does not necessitate its existence, and therefore the removal of mutual truth entails difference — after accepting this, it is only complete if those concepts are taken as existential, like 'thing' and 'possible'. But if they are negative, like 'not a partner of the Creator' and 'no conjunction of contradictories', then there is no way for that to apply. So there is no answer except to restrict the claim to other than the contradictories of those encompassing concepts.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses a subtle logical point about the contradictories of two equal terms. The author first states a general principle: the contradictory of anything is its negation (رفع). Then he argues that if two terms are equal (i.e., they have the same extension), their contradictories must also be equal. For if the contradictories were not equal, there would be a case where one is true and the other false, which would imply that one of the original equal terms is true without the other — a contradiction. This is the main argument. Then he presents a 'strong objection' (شل قوي): the contradictory of 'mutual truth' (تصادق) is simply its negation, i.e., 'not both true', not 'difference' (تفارق). Moreover, sometimes the contradictory of two equal terms may have no instances at all in external reality, like the contradictories of concepts that cover everything (e.g., 'thing' or 'possible'). In such a case, the negation of mutual truth (i.e., that they are not both true of the same subject) could be true even if the contradictories themselves are not equal. For example, consider the concept of 'not having a partner' (لا شريك الباري) — a negative concept that applies to everything except God. Its contradictory, 'having a partner', has no instance in reality. Similarly, 'no conjunction of contradictories' (لا اجتماع النقيضين) — its contradictory is impossible. So the claim that contradictories of equal terms are equal only holds for affirmative concepts that have actual instances. The author then mentions a possible response: someone might say that a negative proposition can be true without the subject existing, but this response only works if the concepts in question are existential (like 'thing' or 'possible'), not if they are negative. Therefore, the only way out is to restrict the original claim to the contradictories of those 'encompassing concepts' that are affirmative. This passage uses purely logical examples, but the principle applies to Islamic legal arguments about contradictory rulings. For instance, if 'wajib' and 'mandub' are not equal, their contradictories cannot be equal.

Key Terms
نقيض
تصادق
تفارق
شل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection about negative concepts like 'لا شريك الباري' challenge the claim that contradictories of equal terms are equal?
A) Because these concepts have no extension at all.
B) Because the contradictory of a negative concept is affirmative and may be impossible, with no instances.
C) Because negative concepts cannot be included in logical relations.
D) Because equal terms can only be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The equality of contradictories holds only for existential (positive) terms; for negative universals, the objection shows the rule must be restricted.

The Inverse Relation of Contradictories Between General and Specific Terms
Author explains the inverse relation and its exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the inverse relation of contradictories between general and specific terms holds and when it fails, and explain why.

Core Idea
Negation of a general term entails negation of its specific, but not vice versa—unless the terms are negative or mixed.

In the previous lesson, we learned the relation between general and specific terms (a'amm wa akhass). Now we turn to their contradictories. A logical principle states that the contradictory of the general implies the contradictory of the specific, but not conversely. However, this principle is not absolute. In this passage, the author presents doubts that arise when the terms are negative or only partially related. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correct reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
The author claims: the contradictory of the general entails the contradictory of the specific, but not conversely. This is because the specific implies the general, so negating the general negates the specific.
The principle is often called 'aks' (inverse) of contradictories.
2
Examine the first doubt
The doubt uses 'no conjunction of contradictories' (a negative universal) as a general term and 'human' as a specific. Their contradictories are 'conjunction of contradictories' (impossible) and 'not human' (possible). These are mutually exclusive (tabayun kulli), so the inverse fails.
The general here is a negative concept (lā ijtimāʿ al-naqīḍayn).
3
Examine the second doubt
With 'general possible' and 'specific possible', the entailment leads to a contradiction: 'every not-general-possible is general possible'. The text says the answer is 'by what has passed' (bimā marr), referring to the distinction between positive and negative concepts.
This shows that the inverse cannot hold when the terms involve possibility and necessity.
4
Understand the resolution
The author clarifies that the inverse relation holds only for positive concepts. When one term is negative, the relation is one of partial mutual exclusion (tabāyun juzʾī). For concepts related 'min wajh' (from one angle), their contradictories also have partial exclusion.
Partial exclusion means some instances of one contradictory overlap with instances of the other.
5
Apply to new cases
Use the criteria to test whether the inverse holds for pairs like 'white' and 'human' (generality from one angle) or 'stone' and 'animal' (mutual exclusion).
For 'white' and 'human', the contradictories 'not white' and 'not human' have partial exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا ، وَنَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصَّ مُطْلَقًا بِالْعَكْسِ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامِّ مَلْزُومٌ لانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاصِ ، وَلَا عَكْسَ؛ تَحْقِيقًا لِمَعْنَى الْعُمُومِ، وَشُكِّكَ بِأَنَّ لَا اجْتِمَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ، مَعَ أَنَّ بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْهِمَا تَبَايُّنَّا كُلِّيًّا، وَأَيْضًا الْمُمْكِنُ الْعَامُ أَعَم مِنَ الْمُمْكِنِ الْخَاصٌ ، فَكُلُّ لَامُمْكِن عَامٌ لَامُمْكِنٌ خَاصٌ ، وَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِن خَاصٌ إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ ، وَكِلَاهُمَا مُمْكِنْ عَامٌ، فَكُل لَا مُمْكِن عَامٌ مُمْكِنْ عَامٌ ، وَالْجَوَابُ بِمَا مَرَّ . وَبَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْ الْأَعَمُ وَالْأَخَصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ تَبَايُنٌ جُزْنِي كَالْمُتَبَابِنَيْنِ، وَهُوَ التَّفَارُقُ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ ؛ لأَنَّ بَيْنَ الْعَيْنَيْنِ تَفَارُقًا، فَحَيْثُ يَصْدُقُ عَيْنُ أَحَدِهِمَا يَصْدُقُ نَقِيضُ الْآخَرِ، وَهُوَ قَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي ضِمْنِ الثَّبَايُنِ الْكُلِّيِّ، كَاللَّا حَجَرِ وَاللَّا حَيَوَانِ، وَالْإِنْسَانِ وَاللَّانَاطِقِ، وَقَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي ضِمْنِ الْعُمُومِ مِنْ وَجْهِ، كَالْأَبْيَض وَالْإِنْسَانِ، وَالْحَجَرِ وَالْحَيَوَانِ. وَهَهُنَا سُوَالٌ وَجَوَابٌ عَلَى طِبْقِ مَا مَرَّ.
And this: the contradictory of the general and the specific absolutely is inverse; for the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific, but not conversely; this is to realize the meaning of generality. And it was doubted because 'no conjunction of contradictories' is more general than 'human', even though between their contradictories there is complete mutual exclusion. Also, 'the general possible' is more general than 'the specific possible'. So every 'not general possible' is a 'not specific possible', and every 'not specific possible' is either necessary or impossible, and both are 'general possible', so every 'not general possible' is 'general possible'. And the answer is by what has passed. And between the contradictories of the general and the specific from one angle there is partial mutual exclusion like two mutually exclusive things, and it is difference in summary; because between the two 'ayns there is difference, so wherever the 'ayn of one of them is true, the contradictory of the other is true. And that may occur within complete mutual exclusion, like 'not stone' and 'not animal', and 'human' and 'not rational'; and it may occur within generality from one angle, like 'white' and 'human', and 'stone' and 'animal'. And here there is a question and answer in line with what has passed.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage examines the relation of the contradictories of a general term (‘āmm) and a specific term (akhass). The author states that the general and specific are 'inverse' with respect to their contradictories: the negation of the general (i.e., the contradictory of the general) entails the negation of the specific (i.e., the contradictory of the specific), but the converse does not hold. For example, if you negate 'animal' (the general), you necessarily negate 'human' (the specific), but negating 'human' does not negate 'animal'. This is the standard logical principle. However, a doubt is raised: take the concept 'no conjunction of contradictories' (لا اجتماع النقيضين) – a negative universal that is extremely general. Its contradictory is 'conjunction of contradictories', which is impossible. The specific 'human' is a positive concept. Their contradictories are mutually exclusive (one is impossible, the other exists). This violates the expected inverse relation because the general here is negative and the specific positive. Another example: 'general possible' (الممكن العام) and 'specific possible' (الممكن الخاص). The specific possible is that which is neither necessary nor impossible; the general possible is that which is not necessary. The contradictory of the general possible (i.e., 'not general possible') includes the necessary and the impossible. The contradictory of the specific possible (i.e., 'not specific possible') also includes the necessary and the impossible. So the alleged entailment leads to a contradiction: every 'not general possible' would be 'general possible'? Actually the text presents a problematic inference. The answer to this doubt is 'by what has passed' (بما مر), presumably the earlier distinction between positive and negative concepts. The author then clarifies that the relation is actually one of 'partial mutual exclusion' (تباين جزئي) between the contradictories of a general and a specific term that have a 'from one angle' (من وجه) relationship. This means the contradictories are true of some things together and false of some things together, but they are not completely opposite. For example, the contradictory of 'not stone' (i.e., stone) and the contradictory of 'not animal' (i.e., animal) are partially exclusive (some stones are animals, some not). Similarly, 'human' and 'not rational' (the contradictory of 'rational') are partially exclusive. This also applies to pairs like 'white' and 'human' (generality from one angle). The passage reinforces the earlier point that the initial inverse relation holds only for positive concepts, and for negative or mixed cases, the relation is more complex.

Key Terms
أعم
أخص
تباين
من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the general term may have no instances.
B) Because one of the terms may be a negative concept.
C) Because the specific term may be a species of a different genus.
D) Because the relation is always reversible.

Key Takeaway
The inverse relation of contradictories holds only when both terms are positive; for negative or mixed terms, the relation is one of partial mutual exclusion.

Direct Conversion (al-‘Aks al-Mustaqīm): Definition and Conditions
Author defines conversion as swapping terms while preserving truth and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of direct conversion to any categorical proposition and determine whether the converse is a necessary consequent.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps terms while preserving truth and quality.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of direct conversion (al-‘aks al-mustaqīm) as presented by the author. Conversion is a type of immediate inference where the subject and predicate of a categorical proposition are exchanged. The key conditions are that the truth value and the quality (affirmative/negative) must be preserved. The author also clarifies that the term 'conversion' may refer to the resulting proposition, provided it is the most specific necessary consequent of the original. This nuance is important for understanding why some conversions are equivalent (e.g., universal negative) while others are not (e.g., universal affirmative).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms of a categorical proposition
Locate the subject (mubtada') and predicate (khabar) in the proposition. For example, in 'No forbidden thing is lawful' (lā ḥarāma ḥalālan), the subject is 'forbidden thing' and the predicate is 'lawful'.
2
Swap the terms to form the converse
Exchange subject and predicate to get 'No lawful thing is forbidden' (lā ḥalāla ḥarāman). This is the converse.
3
Check preservation of truth and quality
Ensure the converse is true if the original is true, and the quality (affirmative/negative) remains the same. Since the original is universal negative (quality: negative), the converse must also be universal negative. Here it is.
For universal affirmative 'All A is B', the converse 'All B is A' is not generally true; the valid converse is 'Some B is A' (particular affirmative), preserving truth and quality.
4
Determine if the converse is the most specific necessary consequent
The resulting proposition should be the strongest that necessarily follows. For universal negative, the converse is also universal negative, so it is the most specific. For universal affirmative, the strongest necessary consequent is particular affirmative.
This condition distinguishes conversion from mere swapping; it ensures logical precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَضْل: الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَقِيمُ وَالْمُسْتَوِي تَبْدِيلُ طَرَفَي الْقَضِيَّةِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالْكَيْفِ ، وَرُبَّمَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْقَضِيَّةِ الْحَاصِلَةِ مِنْهُ ، إِذَا كَانَ أَخَصَّ لَازِمٍ.
Section: Al-‘aks al-mustaqīm and al-mustawī is the exchange of the two terms of a proposition while preserving truth and quality, and it may be applied to the proposition resulting from it, if it is the most specific necessary consequent.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of conversion ('aks) in logic. The author defines two types: al-‘aks al-mustaqīm (direct conversion) and al-‘aks al-mustawī (equable conversion) — essentially the same operation: swapping the subject and predicate of a categorical proposition while preserving its truth value and quality (affirmative/negative). For example, 'No A is B' converts to 'No B is A'. The author also notes that the term 'aks may refer not just to the operation but to the resulting proposition, provided that proposition is the most specific necessary consequent of the original. This implies that conversion may sometimes yield a weaker proposition (e.g., universal affirmative 'All A is B' converts to particular affirmative 'Some B is A'), and it is the 'most specific necessary consequent' meaning that the converse is the strongest proposition that necessarily follows from the original.

Key Terms
الْعَكْس الْمُسْتَقِيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Swapping terms and preserving truth only
B) Swapping terms and preserving quality only
C) Swapping terms while preserving both truth and quality
D) Swapping terms and preserving subject-predicate distinction

Key Takeaway
Direct conversion is exchanging the terms of a categorical proposition while keeping its truth value and quality, and the converse must be the most specific necessary consequent of the original.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Conversion and Universal Negative Rule
Author defines conversion and proves universal negative rule with khulf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define conversion and apply the khulf method to verify the conversion of a universal negative proposition.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps subject and predicate, preserving truth and quality; universal negative proves itself via contradiction.

Conversion is a fundamental immediate inference that transforms propositions while preserving truth. In this lesson, we learn the precise definition of conversion and the method for converting universal negative propositions using proof by contradiction (khulf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential components of conversion
The definition includes: exchange of the two extremes (subject and predicate), preservation of truth, and preservation of quality (affirmative or negative). Note that the result may also be called a 'converse' if it is a necessary consequence.
2
Trace the khulf proof for universal negative
To prove that a universal negative (No A is B) converts as itself (No B is A), assume the converse is false. The falsehood of the converse means its contradictory (Some B is A) is true. Combine this with the original proposition to deduce an impossibility (contradiction). Therefore, the converse must be true.
The text says: 'ضَمُّ نَقِيضِ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ' – combining the contradictory of the converse with the original produces impossibility.
3
Examine the example with body and extension
The example 'Nothing from body is extended infinitely' illustrates a nuance: if the proposition is taken externally (i.e., no body exists that is infinitely extended), the converse is true by empty subject. If taken as a true nature (haqiqi), the converse is false. This shows that conversion depends on the interpretation of the proposition.
External interpretation means the subject term is considered as existing; haqiqi means considering the essence without existence.

فَضْلٌ الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَقِيمُ وَالْمُسْتَوِي تَبْدِيلُ طَرَفَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالْكَيْفِ، وَرُبَّمَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْقَضِيَّةِ الْحَاصِلَةِ مِنْهُ، إِذَا كَانَ أَخَصَّ لَازِمٍ. وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا بِالْخُلْفِ، وَهُوَ هَهُنَا ضَمُّ نَقِيضِ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْمُحَالَ، فَصِدْقُ النَّقِيضِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ مُمْتَنِعٌ، فَيَجِبُ صِدْقُ الْعَكْسِ مَعَهُ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ. وَقَوْلُنَا لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجِسْمِ بِمُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ إِلَى غَيْرِ النَّهَايَةِ لَوْ أُخِذَتْ خَارِجِيَّةً فَعَكْسُهُ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ لِطُولَانِ لَا تَنَاهِي الْأَبْعَادِ. وَإِنْ أُخِذَتْ حَقِيقِيَّةً مَنَعْنَا صِدْقَهَا؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ مُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ جِسْمٌ. وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَوْضُوعِ أَوِ الْمُقَدَّمِ. وَالْمُوجَبَةُ مُطْلَقًا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لِأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ اجْتِمَاعٌ، وَلَا كُلِّيَّةً؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي. وَقَوْلُنَا كُلُّ شَيْخٍ كَانَ شَابًّا الْمَحْمُولُ فِيهِ النِّسْبَةُ، فَعَكْسُهُ بَعْضُ مَنْ كَانَ شَابًّا شَيْخٌ. وَقَوْلُنَا بَعْضُ النَّوْعِ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ؛ لِصِدْقِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِنَوْعٍ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى مَا يُنَاقِضُهُ. وَالسِّرُّ فِيهِ: أَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَفْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ، لَا نَفْسُ مَفْهُومِهِ. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُنْفَصِلَاتِ وَالِاتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ؛ لِعَدَمِ الْجَدْوَى. وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ وَالْعَامَّتَانِ كَنَفْسِهَا، بِالْخُلْفِ. وَالتَّقْرِيبُ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ لَوْلَاهُ لَصَدَقَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ، وَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِإِمْكَانِ صِدْقِ الْإِطْلَاقِ، فَإِنَّا عَنَيْنَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ هَهُنَا الْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمَّ، لَكِنَّ صِدْقَ الْإِطْلَاقِ مُحَالٌ، فَإِمْكَانُهُ مُحَالٌ، فَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُحَالٌ. وَعَلَى هَذَا فَقِسِ الْبَيَانَ فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ كَنِسْبَةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْمُطْلَقَةِ. وَالْمَشْهُورُ أَنَّ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ دَائِمَةً، وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ عُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ.
A section: the straight and simple conversion is the exchange of the two extremes of the proposition while preserving truth and quality, and sometimes it is applied to the proposition resulting from it, if it is a more specific necessary. And the universal negative converts as itself by contradiction, and it is here the combination of the contradictory of the converse with the original, so it produces an impossibility; so the truth of the contradictory together with the original is impossible, so the truth of the converse together with it is necessary, and that is the desired. And our statement 'Nothing from body is extended in all directions to infinity' if taken externally, its converse is true by the subject's non-existence due to the length of the non-finiteness of dimensions. But if taken as true nature, we deny its truth; for everything extended in all directions without limit is a body. And the particular does not convert, due to the possibility of generality of the subject or the antecedent. And the affirmative absolutely converts as particular, because affirmation is conjunction, and not as universal, due to the possibility of generality of the predicate or the consequent. And our statement 'Every old man was young' – the predicate here is the relation, so its converse is 'Some who was young is an old man.' And our statement 'Some of the species is a human' is false, because of the truth of 'Nothing from the human is a species', and it converts to what contradicts it. The secret in it is that what is considered in the common predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate, not the concept itself. And there is no conversion for disjunctives and accidental propositions; due to lack of benefit. And as for according to modality: from the universal negatives, the two perpetuals and the two generales convert as itself, by contradiction. And the approach for the necessary is that if not for it, the possible would be true, and the truth of the possible entails the possibility of the truth of the absolute, for we mean by necessity here the broader sense, but the truth of the absolute is impossible, so its possibility is impossible, so the truth of the possible is impossible. And upon this, analogize the explanation for the general conditional; for the ratio of the possible khiyāniyya to the absolute khiyāniyya is like the ratio of the possible to the absolute. And the well-known is that the necessary converts as perpetual, and the general conditional is a general conventional.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage explains conversion ('aks) of propositions. First, it defines conversion as swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality; the result is called a converse if it is a necessary consequence. Then it gives rules: Universal negative (E) converts as itself (still universal negative) by proof via contradiction (khulf): assume the converse false, combine with original, deduce impossibility. An example: 'Nothing from body is extended infinitely' – if taken externally, converse is true because the subject is empty; if taken as true nature, it is false. Particular propositions do not convert because subject/antecedent might be general. Affirmatives (A and I) convert as particular (I), because affirmation is conjunction. Example: 'Every old man was young' converts to 'Some young was an old man.' Another example: 'Some species is human' is false because 'No human is a species' is true, and this universal negative converts to a contradictory of the original? The author notes the secret: in predication, what matters is truth of the concept of the predicate, not the concept itself. Disjunctives and accidental propositions have no conversion because no benefit. For modal propositions: among universal negatives, the perpetual and general convert as themselves by khulf. For necessary (dururiyya), proof: if conversion false, possible would be true, leading to impossibility. Similarly for general conditional (mashruta 'amma). The well-known view: necessary converts as perpetual, and general conditional as general conventional.

Key Terms
الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَقِيمُ
الْخُلْفُ
السَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the khulf method involve?
A) Combining the original proposition with its converse
B) Combining the contradictory of the converse with the original
C) Swapping subject and predicate without proof
D) Using induction on particular instances

Key Takeaway
Universal negative propositions convert as themselves, proven by the khulf method of combining the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Convert the proposition 'No act of worship is valid without intention' using the khulf method. Write the steps: assume the converse false, combine with original, derive a contradiction, conclude the converse is true.

Conversion of Affirmatives and Particulars
Author explains conversion rules for affirmative and particular propositions, with examples and the underlying secret.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert affirmative propositions correctly into particular affirmatives and explain why particular propositions do not convert.

Core Idea
Affirmatives convert as particular only; particulars do not convert because subject may be general.

While universal negatives convert universally, affirmatives have a different rule. This lesson explains why every affirmative (whether universal or particular) converts as a particular affirmative, and why particular propositions (juz'iyyat) cannot convert at all.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for affirmative propositions
The author says: 'وَالْمُوجَبَةُ مُطْلَقًا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً' – the affirmative absolutely converts as particular. This applies to both universal affirmative (A) and particular affirmative (I).
2
Understand the reason for particular conversion
The reason given is 'لِأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ اجْتِمَاعٌ' – because affirmation signifies conjunction. However, it cannot convert as universal because the predicate (or consequent) might be more general, leading to a false universal converse.
For example, 'Every human is an animal' cannot convert to 'Every animal is a human' because animal is more general.
3
Analyze the example of 'Every old man was young'
This proposition has a relational predicate 'was young'. Its converse is 'Some who was young is an old man' – a particular affirmative, not universal. This demonstrates that even when the predicate is a relation, the conversion rule holds.
4
Examine why particular propositions do not convert
The text says: 'وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ' – the particular does not convert. The reason is the possibility that the subject (or antecedent) is universal, which would make the converse false. For example, 'Some animal is human' does not imply 'Some human is animal' because the converse is also a particular but might be false? Actually, 'Some animal is human' does convert to 'Some human is animal'? That seems true. The author's reasoning is that the subject may be general, but the particular affirmative does convert to a particular affirmative in standard logic. However, the author's point might be about the condition for a valid inference: the converse must be a necessary consequence, and a particular proposition does not guarantee that because the subject may be general. The example given is 'Some species is human' which is false; it converts to something contradictory. The 'secret' mentioned: in common predication, what matters is the truth of the concept of the predicate, not the concept itself. This is a deep point about predication.
The author's claim that particular does not convert is a minority view; many logicians allow conversion of particular affirmatives. The explanation hinges on the nature of predication.

Modal Conversions: Necessity and General Conditionals
Author explains modal conversion rules for perpetual, general, necessary, and general conditional propositions using khulf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion rules to modal propositions like perpetual, general, necessary, and general conditional, and explain the khulf proof for modal conversions.

Core Idea
Modal universal negatives convert preserving their modality via khulf; the necessary converts as perpetual, general conditional as general conventional.

Conversion is not limited to non-modal propositions; it also applies to modal ones. This lesson explains how perpetual and general universal negatives convert as themselves, and how the necessary proposition converts as perpetual, proven by a khulf argument similar to the non-modal case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal types discussed
The author mentions 'the two perpetuals' (al-da'imatan) and 'the two generals' (al-'ammatan) from universal negatives. These refer to modal propositions: perpetual (necessity) and general (conditional). The rule: they convert as themselves (i.e., preserving modality) by khulf.
Perpetual means 'always' or 'necessarily' in a broad sense; general means 'generally' or 'most of the time'.
2
Trace the khulf proof for the necessary (dururiyya)
The proof for the necessary: assume the conversion does not hold (i.e., the converse is false). Then the possible would be true. The truth of the possible entails the possibility of the truth of the absolute (mutlaq). But the truth of the absolute is impossible (because the original is necessary). Therefore, its possibility is impossible, so the truth of the possible is impossible. This contradiction shows that the conversion must hold.
The author says 'الضَّرُورِيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ دَائِمَةً' – the necessary converts as perpetual. This means the converse is also a necessary proposition.
3
Analogize the explanation for the general conditional
The author analogizes the general conditional (mashruta 'amma) to the necessary. The ratio of the possible khiyāniyya to the absolute khiyāniyya is like the ratio of the possible to the absolute. Thus, the same khulf reasoning applies, and the general conditional converts as a general conventional (urfiya 'amma).
Khiyāniyya refers to a kind of conditional proposition? The text uses 'حِينِيَّة' which may be a type of temporal proposition. The exact meaning is technical; the key is that the rule extends.
4
Note the well-known conclusion
The author states the well-known (mashhur) view: the necessary converts as perpetual, and the general conditional converts as general conventional. This is the standard position in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَضْلٌ الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَقِيمُ وَالْمُسْتَوِي تَبْدِيلُ طَرَفَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالْكَيْفِ، وَرُبَّمَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْقَضِيَّةِ الْحَاصِلَةِ مِنْهُ، إِذَا كَانَ أَخَصَّ لَازِمٍ. وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا بِالْخُلْفِ، وَهُوَ هَهُنَا ضَمُّ نَقِيضِ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْمُحَالَ، فَصِدْقُ النَّقِيضِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ مُمْتَنِعٌ، فَيَجِبُ صِدْقُ الْعَكْسِ مَعَهُ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ. وَقَوْلُنَا لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجِسْمِ بِمُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ إِلَى غَيْرِ النَّهَايَةِ لَوْ أُخِذَتْ خَارِجِيَّةً فَعَكْسُهُ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ لِطُولَانِ لَا تَنَاهِي الْأَبْعَادِ. وَإِنْ أُخِذَتْ حَقِيقِيَّةً مَنَعْنَا صِدْقَهَا؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ مُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ جِسْمٌ. وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَوْضُوعِ أَوِ الْمُقَدَّمِ. وَالْمُوجَبَةُ مُطْلَقًا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لِأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ اجْتِمَاعٌ، وَلَا كُلِّيَّةً؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي. وَقَوْلُنَا كُلُّ شَيْخٍ كَانَ شَابًّا الْمَحْمُولُ فِيهِ النِّسْبَةُ، فَعَكْسُهُ بَعْضُ مَنْ كَانَ شَابًّا شَيْخٌ. وَقَوْلُنَا بَعْضُ النَّوْعِ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ؛ لِصِدْقِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِنَوْعٍ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى مَا يُنَاقِضُهُ. وَالسِّرُّ فِيهِ: أَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَفْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ، لَا نَفْسُ مَفْهُومِهِ. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُنْفَصِلَاتِ وَالِاتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ؛ لِعَدَمِ الْجَدْوَى. وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ وَالْعَامَّتَانِ كَنَفْسِهَا، بِالْخُلْفِ. وَالتَّقْرِيبُ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ لَوْلَاهُ لَصَدَقَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ، وَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِإِمْكَانِ صِدْقِ الْإِطْلَاقِ، فَإِنَّا عَنَيْنَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ هَهُنَا الْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمَّ، لَكِنَّ صِدْقَ الْإِطْلَاقِ مُحَالٌ، فَإِمْكَانُهُ مُحَالٌ، فَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُحَالٌ. وَعَلَى هَذَا فَقِسِ الْبَيَانَ فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ كَنِسْبَةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْمُطْلَقَةِ. وَالْمَشْهُورُ أَنَّ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ دَائِمَةً، وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ عُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ.
A section: the straight and simple conversion is the exchange of the two extremes of the proposition while preserving truth and quality, and sometimes it is applied to the proposition resulting from it, if it is a more specific necessary. And the universal negative converts as itself by contradiction, and it is here the combination of the contradictory of the converse with the original, so it produces an impossibility; so the truth of the contradictory together with the original is impossible, so the truth of the converse together with it is necessary, and that is the desired. And our statement 'Nothing from body is extended in all directions to infinity' if taken externally, its converse is true by the subject's non-existence due to the length of the non-finiteness of dimensions. But if taken as true nature, we deny its truth; for everything extended in all directions without limit is a body. And the particular does not convert, due to the possibility of generality of the subject or the antecedent. And the affirmative absolutely converts as particular, because affirmation is conjunction, and not as universal, due to the possibility of generality of the predicate or the consequent. And our statement 'Every old man was young' – the predicate here is the relation, so its converse is 'Some who was young is an old man.' And our statement 'Some of the species is a human' is false, because of the truth of 'Nothing from the human is a species', and it converts to what contradicts it. The secret in it is that what is considered in the common predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate, not the concept itself. And there is no conversion for disjunctives and accidental propositions; due to lack of benefit. And as for according to modality: from the universal negatives, the two perpetuals and the two generales convert as itself, by contradiction. And the approach for the necessary is that if not for it, the possible would be true, and the truth of the possible entails the possibility of the truth of the absolute, for we mean by necessity here the broader sense, but the truth of the absolute is impossible, so its possibility is impossible, so the truth of the possible is impossible. And upon this, analogize the explanation for the general conditional; for the ratio of the possible khiyāniyya to the absolute khiyāniyya is like the ratio of the possible to the absolute. And the well-known is that the necessary converts as perpetual, and the general conditional is a general conventional.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage explains conversion ('aks) of propositions. First, it defines conversion as swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality; the result is called a converse if it is a necessary consequence. Then it gives rules: Universal negative (E) converts as itself (still universal negative) by proof via contradiction (khulf): assume the converse false, combine with original, deduce impossibility. An example: 'Nothing from body is extended infinitely' – if taken externally, converse is true because the subject is empty; if taken as true nature, it is false. Particular propositions do not convert because subject/antecedent might be general. Affirmatives (A and I) convert as particular (I), because affirmation is conjunction. Example: 'Every old man was young' converts to 'Some young was an old man.' Another example: 'Some species is human' is false because 'No human is a species' is true, and this universal negative converts to a contradictory of the original? The author notes the secret: in predication, what matters is truth of the concept of the predicate, not the concept itself. Disjunctives and accidental propositions have no conversion because no benefit. For modal propositions: among universal negatives, the perpetual and general convert as themselves by khulf. For necessary (dururiyya), proof: if conversion false, possible would be true, leading to impossibility. Similarly for general conditional (mashruta 'amma). The well-known view: necessary converts as perpetual, and general conditional as general conventional.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the well-known (mashhur) rule for the conversion of the necessary (dururiyya) proposition?
A) It converts as possible
B) It converts as perpetual
C) It does not convert
D) It converts as particular

Key Takeaway
Modal universal negatives, such as the necessary and general conditional, convert preserving their modality via khulf; the necessary converts as perpetual and the general conditional as general conventional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Convert the modal proposition 'It is necessary that no human is a stone' (universal negative necessary). State the converse and its modality, and outline the khulf proof.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
10 min
Conversion of Universal Negative and Affirmative Propositions
Conversion rules for non-modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of khulf to prove the conversion of a universal negative proposition and explain why particular negatives and universal affirmatives do not convert in their own form.

Core Idea
The universal negative converts as itself via reductio; the affirmative only converts as particular.

This lesson unpacks the core rules of propositional conversion, focusing on the universal negative (No S is P) and the universal affirmative (All S is P). The commentator explains the method of khulf, which uses reductio ad absurdum to prove the converse, and clarifies why particular negatives and universal affirmatives have restricted conversion forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is universal negative, universal affirmative, particular negative, or particular affirmative. The conversion rule depends on this classification.
2
Apply khulf to universal negative
To prove the converse of 'No S is P', assume the contradictory of the converse (some P is S). Conjoin it with the original premise to derive a contradiction (impossibility), showing the converse must be true.
Khulf is a standard method in Islamic logic for immediate inferences.
3
Test universal affirmative conversion
For 'All S is P', the converse 'All P is S' is not always true because the predicate may be broader. The correct converse is 'Some P is S'. The commentator uses the example 'Every old man was a young man' converting to 'Some young were old'.
The affirmative converts as particular, never as universal.
4
Recall exceptions
Particular negatives do not convert because the subject may be universal. Disjunctives and coincidentals have no conversion due to lack of benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا بِالْخُلْفِ، وَهُوَ هَهُنَا ضَمُّ نَقِيضِ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْمُحَالَ، فَصِدْقُ النَّقِيضِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ مُمْتَنِعٌ، فَيَجِبُ صِدْقُ الْعَكْسِ مَعَهُ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ. وَقَوْلُنَا لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجِسْمِ بِمُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ إِلَى غَيْرِ النَّهَايَةِ لَوْ أُخِذَتْ خَارِجِيَّةٌ فَعَكْسُهُ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ؛ لِاطِّلَاعِ [؟] لَا تَنَاهِي الْأَبْعَادِ. وَإِنْ أُخِذَتْ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ مَنَعْنَا صِدْقَهَا؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ مُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ جِسْمٌ. وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، أَوِ الْمُقَدَّمِ. وَالْمُوجَبَةُ مُطْلَقًا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لِأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ اجْتِمَاعٌ، وَلَا كُلِّيَّةً؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي. وَقَوْلُنَا كُلُّ شَيْخٍ كَانَ شَابًّا الْمَحْمُولُ فِيهِ النِّسْبَةُ، فَعَكْسُهُ بَعْضُ مَنْ كَانَ شَابًّا شَيْخٌ. وَقَوْلُنَا بَعْضُ النَّوْعِ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ؛ لِصِدْقِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِنَوْعٍ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى مَا يُنَاقِضُهُ. وَالسِّرُّ فِيهِ: أَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَفْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ، لَا نَفْسُ مَفْهُومِهِ. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُنْفَصِلَاتِ وَالِاتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ؛ لِعَدَمِ الْجَدْوَى. وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ وَالْعَامَّتَانِ كَنَفْسِهَا، بِالْخُلْفِ. وَالتَّقْرِيبُ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ لَوْلَاهُ لَصَدَقَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ، وَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِإِمْكَانِ صِدْقِ الْإِطْلَاقِ، فَإِنَّا عَنَيْنَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ هَهُنَا الْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمَّ، لَكِنَّ صِدْقَ الْإِطْلَاقِ مُحَالٌ، فَإِمْكَانُهُ مُحَالٌ، فَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُحَالٌ. وَعَلَى هَذَا فَقِسِ الْبَيَانَ فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ كَنِسْبَةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْمُطْلَقَةِ. وَالْمَشْهُورُ أَنَّ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ دَائِمَةً، وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ عُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ. وَاسْتُدِلَّ عَلَى انْعِكَاسِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ دَائِمَةً بِأَنَّا إِذَا قَدَّرْنَا أَنَّ مَرْكُوبَ زَيْدٍ مُنْحَصِرٌ فِي الْفَرَسِ، مَعَ إِمْكَانِهِ لِلْحِمَارِ يَصْدُقُ لَا شَيْءَ مِنْ مَرْكُوبِ زَيْدٍ بِحِمَارٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، مَعَ كَذِبٍ عَكْسِهِ الضَّرُورِيِّ. وَيَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ انْفِكَاكُ الدَّوَامِ عَنِ الضَّرُورَةِ فِي الْكُلِّيَّاتِ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا اخْتَلَفُوا فِي انْعِكَاسِ الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ، فَمَنْ يَقُولُ بِانْعِكَاسِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ كَنَفْسِهَا يَقُولُ بِانْعِكَاسِهِمَا كَذَلِكَ، وَمَنْ لَا فَلَا. ثُمَّ الِاخْتِلَافُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ عَلَى رَأْيِ الشَّيْخِ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْفَارَابِي فَمُتَّفَقٌ عَلَى انْعِكَاسِهِمَا كَنَفْسِهِمَا. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ لِلرَّازِي فِي الْمُلَخَّصِ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ الْكِتَابَةَ مُمْكِنَةٌ لِلْإِنْسَانِ، وَالْمُمْكِنُ مُمْكِنُ دَائِمًا، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ الِانْقِلَابُ، فَالسَّلْبُ الدَّائِمُ مُمْكِنٌ، فَلَوْ وَقَعَ مَعَ الْإِنْعِكَاسِ لَصَدَقَ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْكَاتِبِ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَهَذَا مُحَالٌ. وَلَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ فَرْضِ الْمُمْكِنِ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَكُنْ مُمْكِنًا، فَهُوَ مِنَ الْإِنْعِكَاسِ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ دَوَامِ الْإِمْكَانِ إِمْكَانُ الدَّوَامِ. أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْأُمُورِ الْغَيْرِ الْقَارَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّ إِمْكَانَهَا دَائِمٌ، وَدَوَامُهَا غَيْرُ مُمْكِنٍ. وَهَلْ يُشَكُّ فِي أَنَّ بَقَاءَ الْحَرَكَةِ مُحَالٌ لِذَاتِهَا. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَتَبَيَّنُ أَنَّ أَزَلِيَّةَ الْإِمْكَانِ وَإِمْكَانَ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ لَا زَمَانِ. هَذَا، وَالْخَاصَّانِ تَنْعَكِسَانِ عَامَّتَيْنِ، مَعَ اللَّادَوَامِ فِي الْبَعْضِ؛ لِأَنَّ لَادَوَامَ الْأَصْلِ مُوجَبَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، وَهِيَ إِنَّمَا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً. وَلَوْ تَدَبَّرْتَ فِي قَوْلِنَا لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنٍ مَا دَامَ كَاتِبًا لَا دَائِمًا تَيَقَّنْتَ أَنَّهُمَا لَا تَنْعَكِسَانِ كَنَفْسِهِمَا. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْبَوَاقِي، فَإِنَّ أَخَصَّهَا الْوَقْتِيَّةُ، وَهِيَ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى الْمُمْكِنَةِ.
The universal negative converts as itself by reduction to absurdity (khulf), which here is conjoining the contradictory of the converse with the original [premise], producing an impossibility. So the truth of the contradictory with the original is impossible, thus the truth of the converse with it is necessary, which is what is sought. And our saying 'Nothing of body is extended in directions without end'—if it is taken as external [i.e., actual], its converse is true by the non-existence of the subject, due to the knowledge of the non-finiteness of dimensions. And if it is taken as real [genuine], we deny its truth, because everything extended in directions without end is a body. The particular [negative] does not convert, due to the possibility of the subject or the antecedent being universal. The affirmative, absolutely, converts as particular, because affirmation is [a matter of] conjunction, and not as universal, due to the possibility of the predicate or consequent being universal. And our saying 'Every old man was a young man'—the predicate in it is a relation, so its converse is 'Some who were young are old.' And our saying 'Some species is a human' is false, because 'Nothing of human is a species' is true, and it converts to what contradicts it. The secret: what is considered in conventional predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate, not the concept itself. There is no conversion for disjunctives and coincidentals, due to lack of benefit. As for conversion according to modality, of the universal negatives, the two perpetuals (necessity and perpetual possibility) and the two general convert as themselves, by khulf. The approximation in the necessary: if not for it, the possible would be true, and truth of possibility implies possibility of truth of absolute, for we intend by 'necessity' here the broader sense; but truth of absolute is impossible, so its possibility is impossible, so truth of possibility is impossible. And upon this measure the explanation for the general conditional, because the relation of the possible temporal to the absolute temporal is like that of the possible to the absolute. And the well-known [view] is that the necessary converts as perpetual, and the general conditional as general customary. An argument is adduced for the conversion of the necessary as perpetual: if we suppose that Zayd's mount is confined to a horse, while it is possible for him to have a donkey, then 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey' is true necessarily, while its necessary converse is false. And it is objected that this entails the separation of perpetuity from necessity in universals. Hence they differed regarding the conversion of the two possible affirmatives: whoever holds the conversion of the necessary as itself holds their conversion likewise, and whoever does not, does not. Then the difference is only according to the opinion of the Shaykh [Ibn Sina]; but according to the school of al-Farabi, there is agreement on their conversion as themselves. And here there is a doubt by al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas, which is: writing is possible for man, and the possible is always possible, otherwise conversion would follow; so perpetual negation is possible; then if it occurs with conversion, 'Nothing of the writer is a human' would be true, and this is impossible. And does it not follow from the supposition of the possible? Otherwise it would not be possible; so it is from conversion. And its solution: perpetuity of possibility does not imply possibility of perpetuity. Do you not see non-stationary things? For their possibility is perpetual, but their perpetuity is not possible. And is there any doubt that the continuance of motion is impossible by its nature? And hence it becomes clear that the eternity of possibility and the possibility of eternity are not [a matter of] time. This, and the two particular [propositions] convert as general, with non-perpetuity in some, because the non-perpetuity of the original is an absolute affirmative, which only converts as particular. And if you ponder our saying 'Nothing of the writer is a being-at-rest while he is writing, not perpetually,' you become certain that they do not convert as themselves. And there is no conversion for the remaining [modalities], for the most specific of them is the occasional, and it does not convert to the possible.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, explaining the rules of conversion for categorical propositions, especially with modal qualifiers, and resolving a well-known doubt by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The commentator first restates the rule for universal negative conversion by khulf (reductio): conjoin the contradictory of the converse with the original premise to derive an impossibility, proving the converse. An example is given: 'No body is extended infinitely'—if interpreted as an external (actual) proposition, its converse is true because the subject (body) has no infinite extension; if interpreted as a real (essential) proposition, it is false because any infinite extension is a body. The particular negative does not convert because the subject may be universal. The affirmative converts as particular (e.g., 'Every old man was young' becomes 'Some young were old'), not universal, because the predicate could be broader. A subtle point: 'Some species is human' is false because 'No human is a species' is true and converts to a contradictory. The secret is that conventional predication considers truth of the predicate's concept, not the concept itself. Disjunctives and coincidental propositions have no conversion because it is useless.

Turning to modal conversion, the commentator explains that universal negative modal propositions (necessary, perpetual, general) convert as themselves via khulf. For necessity: if the necessary universal negative did not convert, the possible negative would be true, but that leads to impossibility. The well-known view is that the necessary converts as perpetual, and the general conditional as general customary. A counterargument is presented: suppose Zayd's mount is restricted to a horse but could be a donkey—then 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey' is necessarily true, but its necessary converse is false; this shows that perpetuity cannot be separated from necessity in universals. This leads to disagreement about the conversion of two possible affirmatives: those who accept the necessary conversion accept theirs, and those who reject do not. The difference is only on Ibn Sina's view; on al-Farabi's view, there is consensus.

Al-Razi's doubt in al-Mulakhkhas: writing is possible for man, and the possible is always possible, so a perpetual negation is possible. If that perpetual negation occurs with conversion, 'Nothing of the writer is human' would be true—impossible. The commentator solves it: perpetuity of possibility does not imply possibility of perpetuity, as seen in non-stationary accidents like motion—their possibility is perpetual but their perpetuity is impossible. Thus, the eternity of possibility and possibility of eternity are not temporal. Finally, the two particular modal propositions convert as general, with non-perpetuity in some, because the non-perpetuity of the original is an absolute affirmative that converts only as particular. The remaining modalities (like occasional) have no conversion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the universal negative proposition convert as itself using the method of khulf?
A) Because the subject and predicate are both universal.
B) Because conjoining the contradictory of the converse with the original leads to an impossibility, proving the converse.
C) Because the predicate is always broader than the subject.
D) Because the original is a necessary proposition.

Key Takeaway
The universal negative always converts as itself via khulf; the universal affirmative converts only as particular; particular negatives, disjunctives, and coincidentals have no valid conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'No horse is a donkey' (universal negative). Use khulf to prove its converse. Write out the steps assuming the contradictory and deriving an impossibility.
?
Convert the universal affirmative 'All human beings are mortal' into its correct converse form. Explain why it cannot be universal.
