Modal Conversion and Al-Razi's Doubt
Conversion of modal propositions and resolution of al-Razi's doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which modal propositions convert and resolve al-Razi's doubt concerning the conversion of possible propositions.

Core Idea
Possibility of perpetuity does not follow from perpetuity of possibility.

Modal propositions add the dimensions of necessity, perpetuity, and possibility. The commentator explains that universal negative modal propositions (necessity, perpetual, general) convert as themselves, but there is a subtle distinction between necessity and perpetuity. Al-Razi's doubt about possible propositions is resolved by distinguishing between the eternity of possibility and the possibility of eternity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal types
The passage discusses necessary (daruriyya), perpetual (da'ima), general ('amma), possible (mumkina), and particular (khassa) modal propositions. Note their definitions.
Necessary here means perpetual (broad sense).
2
Recall conversion rules for necessity and perpetual
Universal negative modal propositions (necessary, perpetual, general) convert as themselves via khulf. The commentator provides a proof: if the necessary negative did not convert, the possible negative would be true, leading to impossibility.
3
Analyze al-Razi's doubt
Al-Razi argues: writing is possible for man, and the possible is always possible; so a perpetual negation is possible. If that perpetual negation occurs with conversion, 'Nothing of the writer is human' would be true—impossible. The conclusion seems to follow, but the commentator shows the error.
4
Apply the resolution
The key distinction: perpetuity of possibility does not imply possibility of perpetuity. Example: motion's possibility is perpetual, but its perpetuity is impossible. Hence al-Razi's inference that a perpetual negation is possible (from the possibility of writing) is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ تَنْعَكِسُ كَنَفْسِهَا بِالْخُلْفِ، وَهُوَ هَهُنَا ضَمُّ نَقِيضِ الْعَكْسِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْمُحَالَ، فَصِدْقُ النَّقِيضِ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ مُمْتَنِعٌ، فَيَجِبُ صِدْقُ الْعَكْسِ مَعَهُ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ. وَقَوْلُنَا لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجِسْمِ بِمُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ إِلَى غَيْرِ النَّهَايَةِ لَوْ أُخِذَتْ خَارِجِيَّةٌ فَعَكْسُهُ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ؛ لِاطِّلَاعِ [؟] لَا تَنَاهِي الْأَبْعَادِ. وَإِنْ أُخِذَتْ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ مَنَعْنَا صِدْقَهَا؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ مُمْتَدٍّ فِي الْجِهَاتِ لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ جِسْمٌ. وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، أَوِ الْمُقَدَّمِ. وَالْمُوجَبَةُ مُطْلَقًا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً، لِأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ اجْتِمَاعٌ، وَلَا كُلِّيَّةً؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي. وَقَوْلُنَا كُلُّ شَيْخٍ كَانَ شَابًّا الْمَحْمُولُ فِيهِ النِّسْبَةُ، فَعَكْسُهُ بَعْضُ مَنْ كَانَ شَابًّا شَيْخٌ. وَقَوْلُنَا بَعْضُ النَّوْعِ إِنْسَانٌ كَاذِبٌ؛ لِصِدْقِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِنَوْعٍ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى مَا يُنَاقِضُهُ. وَالسِّرُّ فِيهِ: أَنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَفْهُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ، لَا نَفْسُ مَفْهُومِهِ. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُنْفَصِلَاتِ وَالِاتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ؛ لِعَدَمِ الْجَدْوَى. وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ وَالْعَامَّتَانِ كَنَفْسِهَا، بِالْخُلْفِ. وَالتَّقْرِيبُ فِي الضَّرُورِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ لَوْلَاهُ لَصَدَقَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ، وَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِإِمْكَانِ صِدْقِ الْإِطْلَاقِ، فَإِنَّا عَنَيْنَا بِالضَّرُورَةِ هَهُنَا الْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمَّ، لَكِنَّ صِدْقَ الْإِطْلَاقِ مُحَالٌ، فَإِمْكَانُهُ مُحَالٌ، فَصِدْقُ الْإِمْكَانِ مُحَالٌ. وَعَلَى هَذَا فَقِسِ الْبَيَانَ فِي الْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ كَنِسْبَةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ إِلَى الْمُطْلَقَةِ. وَالْمَشْهُورُ أَنَّ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ تَنْعَكِسُ دَائِمَةً، وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ عُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ. وَاسْتُدِلَّ عَلَى انْعِكَاسِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ دَائِمَةً بِأَنَّا إِذَا قَدَّرْنَا أَنَّ مَرْكُوبَ زَيْدٍ مُنْحَصِرٌ فِي الْفَرَسِ، مَعَ إِمْكَانِهِ لِلْحِمَارِ يَصْدُقُ لَا شَيْءَ مِنْ مَرْكُوبِ زَيْدٍ بِحِمَارٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، مَعَ كَذِبٍ عَكْسِهِ الضَّرُورِيِّ. وَيَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ انْفِكَاكُ الدَّوَامِ عَنِ الضَّرُورَةِ فِي الْكُلِّيَّاتِ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا اخْتَلَفُوا فِي انْعِكَاسِ الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ، فَمَنْ يَقُولُ بِانْعِكَاسِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ كَنَفْسِهَا يَقُولُ بِانْعِكَاسِهِمَا كَذَلِكَ، وَمَنْ لَا فَلَا. ثُمَّ الِاخْتِلَافُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ عَلَى رَأْيِ الشَّيْخِ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْفَارَابِي فَمُتَّفَقٌ عَلَى انْعِكَاسِهِمَا كَنَفْسِهِمَا. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ لِلرَّازِي فِي الْمُلَخَّصِ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ الْكِتَابَةَ مُمْكِنَةٌ لِلْإِنْسَانِ، وَالْمُمْكِنُ مُمْكِنُ دَائِمًا، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ الِانْقِلَابُ، فَالسَّلْبُ الدَّائِمُ مُمْكِنٌ، فَلَوْ وَقَعَ مَعَ الْإِنْعِكَاسِ لَصَدَقَ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْكَاتِبِ بِإِنْسَانٍ، وَهَذَا مُحَالٌ. وَلَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ فَرْضِ الْمُمْكِنِ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَكُنْ مُمْكِنًا، فَهُوَ مِنَ الْإِنْعِكَاسِ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ دَوَامِ الْإِمْكَانِ إِمْكَانُ الدَّوَامِ. أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْأُمُورِ الْغَيْرِ الْقَارَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّ إِمْكَانَهَا دَائِمٌ، وَدَوَامُهَا غَيْرُ مُمْكِنٍ. وَهَلْ يُشَكُّ فِي أَنَّ بَقَاءَ الْحَرَكَةِ مُحَالٌ لِذَاتِهَا. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَتَبَيَّنُ أَنَّ أَزَلِيَّةَ الْإِمْكَانِ وَإِمْكَانَ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ لَا زَمَانِ. هَذَا، وَالْخَاصَّانِ تَنْعَكِسَانِ عَامَّتَيْنِ، مَعَ اللَّادَوَامِ فِي الْبَعْضِ؛ لِأَنَّ لَادَوَامَ الْأَصْلِ مُوجَبَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، وَهِيَ إِنَّمَا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةً. وَلَوْ تَدَبَّرْتَ فِي قَوْلِنَا لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنٍ مَا دَامَ كَاتِبًا لَا دَائِمًا تَيَقَّنْتَ أَنَّهُمَا لَا تَنْعَكِسَانِ كَنَفْسِهِمَا. وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْبَوَاقِي، فَإِنَّ أَخَصَّهَا الْوَقْتِيَّةُ، وَهِيَ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى الْمُمْكِنَةِ.
The universal negative converts as itself by reduction to absurdity (khulf), which here is conjoining the contradictory of the converse with the original [premise], producing an impossibility. So the truth of the contradictory with the original is impossible, thus the truth of the converse with it is necessary, which is what is sought. And our saying 'Nothing of body is extended in directions without end'—if it is taken as external [i.e., actual], its converse is true by the non-existence of the subject, due to the knowledge of the non-finiteness of dimensions. And if it is taken as real [genuine], we deny its truth, because everything extended in directions without end is a body. The particular [negative] does not convert, due to the possibility of the subject or the antecedent being universal. The affirmative, absolutely, converts as particular, because affirmation is [a matter of] conjunction, and not as universal, due to the possibility of the predicate or consequent being universal. And our saying 'Every old man was a young man'—the predicate in it is a relation, so its converse is 'Some who were young are old.' And our saying 'Some species is a human' is false, because 'Nothing of human is a species' is true, and it converts to what contradicts it. The secret: what is considered in conventional predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate, not the concept itself. There is no conversion for disjunctives and coincidentals, due to lack of benefit. As for conversion according to modality, of the universal negatives, the two perpetuals (necessity and perpetual possibility) and the two general convert as themselves, by khulf. The approximation in the necessary: if not for it, the possible would be true, and truth of possibility implies possibility of truth of absolute, for we intend by 'necessity' here the broader sense; but truth of absolute is impossible, so its possibility is impossible, so truth of possibility is impossible. And upon this measure the explanation for the general conditional, because the relation of the possible temporal to the absolute temporal is like that of the possible to the absolute. And the well-known [view] is that the necessary converts as perpetual, and the general conditional as general customary. An argument is adduced for the conversion of the necessary as perpetual: if we suppose that Zayd's mount is confined to a horse, while it is possible for him to have a donkey, then 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey' is true necessarily, while its necessary converse is false. And it is objected that this entails the separation of perpetuity from necessity in universals. Hence they differed regarding the conversion of the two possible affirmatives: whoever holds the conversion of the necessary as itself holds their conversion likewise, and whoever does not, does not. Then the difference is only according to the opinion of the Shaykh [Ibn Sina]; but according to the school of al-Farabi, there is agreement on their conversion as themselves. And here there is a doubt by al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas, which is: writing is possible for man, and the possible is always possible, otherwise conversion would follow; so perpetual negation is possible; then if it occurs with conversion, 'Nothing of the writer is a human' would be true, and this is impossible. And does it not follow from the supposition of the possible? Otherwise it would not be possible; so it is from conversion. And its solution: perpetuity of possibility does not imply possibility of perpetuity. Do you not see non-stationary things? For their possibility is perpetual, but their perpetuity is not possible. And is there any doubt that the continuance of motion is impossible by its nature? And hence it becomes clear that the eternity of possibility and the possibility of eternity are not [a matter of] time. This, and the two particular [propositions] convert as general, with non-perpetuity in some, because the non-perpetuity of the original is an absolute affirmative, which only converts as particular. And if you ponder our saying 'Nothing of the writer is a being-at-rest while he is writing, not perpetually,' you become certain that they do not convert as themselves. And there is no conversion for the remaining [modalities], for the most specific of them is the occasional, and it does not convert to the possible.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, explaining the rules of conversion for categorical propositions, especially with modal qualifiers, and resolving a well-known doubt by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The commentator first restates the rule for universal negative conversion by khulf (reductio): conjoin the contradictory of the converse with the original premise to derive an impossibility, proving the converse. An example is given: 'No body is extended infinitely'—if interpreted as an external (actual) proposition, its converse is true because the subject (body) has no infinite extension; if interpreted as a real (essential) proposition, it is false because any infinite extension is a body. The particular negative does not convert because the subject may be universal. The affirmative converts as particular (e.g., 'Every old man was young' becomes 'Some young were old'), not universal, because the predicate could be broader. A subtle point: 'Some species is human' is false because 'No human is a species' is true and converts to a contradictory. The secret is that conventional predication considers truth of the predicate's concept, not the concept itself. Disjunctives and coincidental propositions have no conversion because it is useless.

Turning to modal conversion, the commentator explains that universal negative modal propositions (necessary, perpetual, general) convert as themselves via khulf. For necessity: if the necessary universal negative did not convert, the possible negative would be true, but that leads to impossibility. The well-known view is that the necessary converts as perpetual, and the general conditional as general customary. A counterargument is presented: suppose Zayd's mount is restricted to a horse but could be a donkey—then 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey' is necessarily true, but its necessary converse is false; this shows that perpetuity cannot be separated from necessity in universals. This leads to disagreement about the conversion of two possible affirmatives: those who accept the necessary conversion accept theirs, and those who reject do not. The difference is only on Ibn Sina's view; on al-Farabi's view, there is consensus.

Al-Razi's doubt in al-Mulakhkhas: writing is possible for man, and the possible is always possible, so a perpetual negation is possible. If that perpetual negation occurs with conversion, 'Nothing of the writer is human' would be true—impossible. The commentator solves it: perpetuity of possibility does not imply possibility of perpetuity, as seen in non-stationary accidents like motion—their possibility is perpetual but their perpetuity is impossible. Thus, the eternity of possibility and possibility of eternity are not temporal. Finally, the two particular modal propositions convert as general, with non-perpetuity in some, because the non-perpetuity of the original is an absolute affirmative that converts only as particular. The remaining modalities (like occasional) have no conversion.

Key Terms
الضَّرُورِيَّةُ
الدَّائِمَةُ
العَامَّةُ
المُمْكِنَةُ
الْخَاصَّانِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Perpetuity of possibility does not imply possibility of perpetuity.
C) Necessity and perpetuity are identical.
D) The possible never converts.

Key Takeaway
Modal conversion rules require careful distinction between necessity, perpetuity, and possibility; al-Razi's doubt is resolved by recognizing that perpetuity of possibility does not entail possibility of perpetuity.

Conversion of Necessary Perpetual Propositions: Proof and Objection
Proof and objection for conversion of necessary perpetual proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the proof for converting a necessary perpetual proposition and identify the objection that challenges its validity.

Core Idea
A necessary perpetual proposition does not always convert with necessity preserved.

This passage presents a proof that a necessary perpetual proposition (e.g., 'No Zayd's mount is a donkey necessarily') can be converted to a necessary proposition. The author then raises an objection: the proof leads to the disjunction of perpetuity from necessity in universals. Understanding this exchange sharpens your grasp of modal conversion in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial proposition
The proof starts with a supposition: Zayd's mount is restricted to horse, but it is possible for him to ride a donkey. What is the proposition being tested?
2
Identify the original necessary proposition
'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey' is true necessarily. This is the original proposition.
3
Determine its converse
The necessary converse would be 'No donkey is a mount of Zayd'. Is this true? No, because Zayd could possibly ride a donkey.
4
Recognize the objection
The objection states that this proof leads to disjoining perpetuity from necessity in universals. Why is that problematic? Because a necessary perpetual should have both properties together.
5
Assess the impact on conversion
The objection suggests that the proof fails because it shows the necessity is not truly perpetual; thus conversion of necessary perpetual propositions is not generally valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاسْتُدِلَّ عَلَى انْعِكَاسِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ دَائِمَةٌ بِأَنَّا إِذَا قَدَّرْنَا أَنَّ مَرْكُوبَ زَيْدٍ مُنْحَصِرٌ فِي الْفَرَسِ ، مَعَ إِمْكَانِهِ لِلْحِمَارِ يَصْدُقُ لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ مَرْكُوبِ زَيْدٍ بِحِمَارِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ ، مَعَ كَذِبٍ عَكْسِهِ الضَّرُورِيُّ. وَيَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ انْفِكَاكُ الدَّوَامِ عَنِ الضَّرُورَةِ فِي الكليات .
And a proof is given for the conversion of the necessary perpetual: that if we suppose that Zayd's mount is restricted to horse, with it being possible for the donkey, then 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey' is true necessarily, while its necessary converse is false. And it is objected against it that the disjunction of perpetuity from necessity in universals follows.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the conversion of a necessary perpetual proposition (الضرورية الدائمة), i.e., a universal affirmative necessary proposition. The proof uses the example: if Zayd's mount is only a horse, but it is possible for him to ride a donkey (i.e., it is not necessarily a horse), then the proposition 'No mount of Zayd is a donkey' is true necessarily. Its converse, 'No donkey is a mount of Zayd', would be false because donkeys can possibly be mounted by Zayd. The objection is that this proof entails that in universal propositions, perpetuity (الدوام) is separated from necessity (الضرورة), meaning the necessity is not perpetual, which is problematic.

Key Terms
الضرورية الدائمة
الانعكاس

Key Takeaway
The conversion of necessary perpetual propositions is not straightforward; the objection shows that the necessity must be truly perpetual for conversion to hold.

Dispute on Conversion of Possible Affirmatives: Al-Razi's Doubt and Solution
Author explains the disagreement on conversion of two possible affirmatives and presents al-Razi's doubt and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule to possible affirmative propositions and identify the logical condition that resolves al-Razi's paradox.

Core Idea
A possible proposition's conversion requires distinguishing perpetual possibility from possibility of perpetuity.

In this passage, the author discusses a disagreement among logicians about whether the conversion rule for necessary propositions also applies to possible affirmative propositions. Al-Razi presents a doubt that seems to show a contradiction if conversion holds, but the solution reveals a subtle distinction between perpetual possibility and the possibility of perpetuity. By understanding this distinction, you will grasp a key nuance in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible affirmatives
The passage refers to universal and particular possible affirmatives (e.g., 'Every A is possibly B' and 'Some A is possibly B'). Understand what the disagreement is about: whether these propositions convert like necessary ones.
2
Understand al-Razi's doubt
Al-Razi assumes the example 'Writing is possible for man'. He argues that if conversion holds, then from 'Some man is possibly a writer' we get 'Some writer is possibly a man'. But if the perpetual negative 'No writer is a man' is also possible (which al-Razi assumes follows from perpetual possibility of the affirmative), then absurdly 'Nothing of the writer is a man' would be true. Trace the logical steps in the text.
The doubt is that if conversion yields a possible affirmative, the corresponding negative might also be possible, leading to contradiction.
3
Analyze the solution's key distinction
The solution states that perpetual possibility does not entail possibility of perpetuity. For example, motion is always possible but cannot be perpetual. Al-Razi's doubt confuses these two notions. Apply this to the writing example: writing is perpetually possible for man, but that does not mean writing can continue forever (which would make the negative impossible).
The Arabic phrase 'لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ دَوَامِ الْإِمْكَانِ إِمْكَانُ الدَّوَامِ' captures the core distinction.
4
Check validity of conversion for possible propositions
The conversion of a possible affirmative ('Some A is possibly B') to 'Some B is possibly A' is valid, as agreed by al-Farabi and most logicians. Al-Razi's doubt does not invalidate the conversion; it only highlights a subtle confusion. Verify with your own examples from kalam or fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ هَهُنَا اخْتَلَفُوا فِي الْعِكَاسِ الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ، فَمَنْ يَقُولُ بِالْعِكَاسِ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ كَنَفْسِهَا يَقُولُ بِالْعِكَاسِهِمَا كَذَلِكَ، وَمَنْ لَا فَلَا ثُمَّ الاِخْتِلَافُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ عَلَى رَأْي الشَّيْخ ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْفَارَابِي فَمُتَّفَقٌ عَلَى انْعِكَاسِهِمَا كَنَفْسِهِمَا. وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ لِلرَّازِي فِي الْمُلَخَصِ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ الْكِتَابَةَ مُمْكِنَةٌ لِلْإِنْسَانِ ، وَالْمُمْكِنُ مُمْكِنُ دَائِمًا، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ الاِنْقِلابُ ، فَالسَّلْبُ الدَّائِمُ مُمْكِنٌ، فَلَوْ وَقَعَ مَعَ الإِنْعِكَاسِ لَصَدَقَ لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْكَاتِبِ بِإِنْسَانٍ» ، وَهَذَا مُحَالٌ ، وَلَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ فَرْضِ الْمُمْكِنِ ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَكُنْ مُمْكِنًا ، فَهُوَ مِنَ الاِنْعِكَاس.(( وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ دَوَامِ الْإِمْكَانِ إِمْكَانُ الدَّوَامِ. أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْأُمُورِ الْغَيْرِ الْقَارَّةِ ؛ فَإِنَّ إِمْكَانَهَا دَائِمٌ ، وَدَوَامُهَا غَيْرُ مُمْكِنٍ، وَهَلْ يُشَكُ فِي أَنَّ بَقَاءَ الْحَرَكَةِ مُحَالٌ لِذَاتِهَا. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَتَبَيَّنُ أَنَّ أَزَلِيَّةَ الْإِمْكَانِ وَإِمْكَانَ الْأَزَلِيَّةِ لاَ لا زَمَانِ .
And from here they differed regarding the conversion of the two possible affirmatives. He who holds the conversion of the necessary as itself holds their conversion likewise, and he who does not does not. Then the difference is only according to the opinion of the Shaykh; as for the school of al-Farabi, it is agreed upon their conversion as themselves. And here is a doubt of al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhas: that writing is possible for man, and the possible is possible perpetually; otherwise change would follow. So the perpetual negative is possible. If it occurs with conversion, then 'Nothing of the writer is a man' would be true, and this is absurd. And it does not follow from the supposition of the possible; otherwise it would not be possible. So it is from the conversion. And its solution: that perpetual possibility does not entail possibility of perpetuity. Do you not see non-stationary things? For their possibility is perpetual, yet their perpetuity is not possible. Is there any doubt that the continuation of motion is impossible in itself? And from this it becomes clear that the eternity of possibility and the possibility of eternity are not [related]? there is no time.

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of two possible affirmatives (الممكنتين الموجبتين), i.e., universal and particular possible propositions. The author notes the disagreement: those who accept conversion of necessary propositions also accept conversion of possible ones, and vice versa. The difference is attributed to Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh), while al-Farabi's school agrees on their conversion. Then al-Razi raises a doubt: take the proposition 'Writing is possible for man'. Since the possible is perpetually possible, 'Nothing of the writer is a man' would be a perpetual negative possible? Actually al-Razi's argument is: if we convert the possible affirmative 'Some man is a writer' (which is possible) to 'Some writer is a man' (possible), but then the negative 'Nothing of the writer is a man' would be false? Wait, the text is: 'فَالسَّلْبُ الدَّائِمُ مُمْكِنٌ' - the perpetual negative is possible. Then if conversion holds, 'Nothing of the writer is a man' would be true (because it is the converse? Actually it's the converse of what? This is confusing. The commentary should explain al-Razi's doubt: he assumes that if 'Some man is a writer' is possible, then its converse 'Some writer is a man' is possible. But then the negative 'No writer is a man' would be false. However, if the perpetual negative is possible, that would contradict. The solution is that perpetual possibility (writing is always possible for man) does not entail possibility of perpetuity (that writing continues forever). The example of motion: motion is always possible but cannot be perpetual. Thus the doubt is resolved.

Conversion of Special Propositions and Negative Particulars
Author states the conversion rules for the two special propositions and for negative particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which special propositions convert and explain the conversion rule for negative particulars using the condition of mutually exclusive predicates.

Core Idea
Not every negative particular converts; only those with mutually exclusive predicates do.

In this passage, the author delves into the conversion of special propositions—those with exclusive conditions—and clarifies the rule for negative particulars. You will learn that only among negative particulars, the two specials (with mutually exclusive predicates) convert as themselves, while others do not. Let's unpack the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special propositions
Special propositions (الخاصتان) are those that include an exclusive condition, e.g., 'No writer is stationary as long as he writes.' They are not simple universal or particular.
Look for phrases that imply a condition like 'as long as' or 'except when'.
2
Understand their conversion rule
The author says they convert as universals (عامتين) but with non-perpetuity (لادوام) in some. This means the converse is a universal proposition that does not claim perpetual truth.
Example: 'No writer is stationary as long as he writes' converts to 'No stationary thing writes (at the moment of stillness).'
3
Examine the counterexample for 'as themselves'
The author shows they do not convert 'as themselves' (كنفسهما) — the converse is not identical in form because the condition flips.
Contemplate the example: 'Nothing of the writer is stationary as long as he writes, not perpetually' — its converse is not 'Nothing stationary is a writer as long as it is stationary.'
4
State the rule for negative particulars
Among negative particular propositions (السوالب الجزئية), only the two specials (the ones with the exclusive condition) convert. Other negative particulars do not convert at all.
This is explicitly stated: 'وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَّا الْخَاصَّتَانِ'
5
Reason with mutually exclusive predicates
The two specials have predicates that are mutually exclusive in one essence according to the first part, but they come together in the subject according to the second part. This mutual exclusivity ensures that if something is not B as long as it is C, it is also not C as long as it is B.
The variables (ب) and (ج) represent these mutually exclusive predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا ، وَالْخَاصَّنَانِ تَنْعَكِسَانِ عَامَّتَيْنِ ، مَعَ اللَّادَوَامِ فِي الْبَعْضِ ؛ لِأَنَّ لَادَوَامَ الأَصْل مُوجَبَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ ، وَهِيَ إِنَّمَا تَنْعَكِسُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، وَلَوْ تَدَبَّرْتَ فِي قَوْلِنَا لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِن مَا دَامَ كَاتِبًا لَا دَائِمًا تَيَقَّنْتَ أَنَّهُمَا لَا تَنْعَكِسَانِ كَنَفْسِهِمَا، وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْبَوَاقِي ، فَإِنَّ أَخَصَّهَا الْوَقْتِيَّةُ ، وَهِيَ لا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى الْمُمْكِنَةِ ؛ لِصِدْقِ «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفَ بِالتَّوْقِيتِ، لَا دَائِمًا، مَعَ كَذِبِ بَعْضُ الْمُنْخَسِفِ لَيْسَ بِقَمَرِ بِالْإِمْكَانِ . وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَّا الْخَاصَّتَانِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُمَا تَنْعَكِسَانِ كَنَفْسِهِمَا ؛ لِأَنَّ الْوَصْفَيْنِ مُتَنَافِيَانِ فِي ذَاتٍ وَاحِدَةٍ بِحُكْم الْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ، وَقَدْ اجْتَمَعَا فِيهَا بِحُكْمِ الْجُزْءِ الثَّانِي، فَتِلْكَ الذَّاتُ كَمَا لَمْ تَكُنْ (ب) مَا دَامَ (ج) لَا تَكُونُ (ج) مَا دَامَ (ب) ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ .
This, and the two specials convert as universals, with non-perpetuity in some; because the non-perpetuity of the original is an absolute affirmative, and it converts as a particular. And if you contemplate our saying 'Nothing of the writer is a stationary thing as long as he is writing, not perpetually', you will be certain that they do not convert as themselves. And there is no conversion for the remaining, for the most specific of them is the temporally conditioned, and it does not convert to the possible, due to the truth of 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed temporally, not perpetually', while the converse 'Some eclipse is not a moon' is false as possible. And of the negative particulars, only the two specials convert; for they convert as themselves, because the two descriptions are mutually exclusive in one essence according to the first part, and they have come together in it according to the second part. So that essence, just as it is not (B) as long as (C), it is not (C) as long as (B), which is desired.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion of special propositions (خاصتان) — these are probably propositions with exclusive conditions like 'No A is B except when C'. The author says they convert as universals (عامتين) but with non-perpetuity in some cases. For example, 'No writer is stationary as long as he writes' is a special proposition; its converse is not the same. Then he discusses negative particulars: among them, only the two specials convert as themselves. The reason is that the two predicates are mutually exclusive in one subject according to the first clause but have come together in the subject according to the second clause, so the contrapositive relationship holds. The example of the lunar eclipse: 'No moon is eclipsed temporarily (without perpetuity)' is true, but its possible converse 'Some eclipse is not a moon' is false.

Key Terms
الْخَاصَّتَانِ
السَّوَالِبُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ
اللَّادَوَامُ
الْوَقْتِيَّةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) None

Key Takeaway
Among negative particulars, only the two special propositions—with mutually exclusive predicates—convert, and they convert as themselves due to the reciprocal relationship of their conditions.

Conversion of Affirmative Propositions by Reductio and Assumption
Author lists convertible affirmatives and explains the method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of reductio and assumption to convert affirmative propositions and identify which propositions are convertible.

Core Idea
Reductio and assumption convert affirmative propositions without direct inference.

In this passage, the author explains that certain affirmative propositions convert by a method called reductio (al-khalf) and assumption (al-iftirad). This is not a simple swap of subject and predicate; it involves a logical demonstration using a syllogism and contradiction. Understanding this method is crucial for immediate inferences in logic and their application in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the convertible affirmatives
The passage lists two existential propositions (universal and particular existential), two temporal propositions, and the universal absolute. Note that these are the affirmative propositions that convert.
Existential propositions are about existence; temporal about time; universal absolute is unrestricted.
2
Understand the assumption method
The author states: 'We assume (C) which is (B) is (D)'. This means we posit an individual D that has both properties B (subject description) and C (predicate description). The key is that D is a concrete instance of the subject and predicate.
Variables: originally (ج) for C, (ب) for B, (د) for D. In translation: assume D which is B is C.
3
Recognize the third figure syllogism
From 'D is B' and 'D is C', we infer 'some B is C' via the third figure (disamis). This gives us the converse: some B is C.
The third figure has the middle term as subject of both premises.
4
Understand the role of contradiction
The method also uses reductio: the contradictory of the converse is assumed and shown to contradict the original proposition, thus proving the converse.
This ensures the conversion is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنَ الْمُوجَبَاتِ تَنْعَكِسُ الْوُجُودِيَّتَانِ وَالْوَقْتِيَتَانِ وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ مُطْلَقَةٌ عَامَّةً ، بِالْخُلْفِ وَالِافْتِرَاضِ ، وَهُوَ أَنْ نَفْرِضَ ذَاتَ الْمَوْضُوعِ شَيْئًا وَيُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهِ وَصْفُ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَصْفُ الْمَحْمُولِ ، فَتَقُولُ: نَفْرِضُ (ج) الَّذِي هُوَ (ب) (د) ، ف(د) (ب)، و(د) (ج) ، فَبَعْضُ (ب) (ج) بِالْفِعْلِ مِنَ الثَّالِثِ، وَالْعَكْسِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَنْعَكِسَ نَقِيضُ الْعَكْسِ لِيَرْتَدَّ إِلَى مَا يُنَافِي الْأَصْلَ. وَالدَّائِمَتَانِ وَالْعَامَّتَانِ حِينِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ ، بِالْوُجُوهِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ.
And of the affirmatives, the two existential, the two temporal, and the universal absolute convert as a universal absolute, by reductio and assumption. That is, we assume a thing of the subject and predicate the description of the subject and the description of the predicate, so we say: we assume (C) which is (B) is (D). So (D) is (B), and (D) is (C), so some (B) is (C) actually from the third figure and from conversion. And it is that the contradictory of the converse converts to revert to what contradicts the original. And the two perpetuals and the two universals convert as a temporally conditioned absolute, by the mentioned methods.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage lists which affirmative propositions convert. The existential (وجوديتان) and temporal (وقتيتان) and the universal absolute (مطلقة عامة) convert as a universal absolute. The method is by reductio (الخلف) and assumption (الافتراض). The author explains the assumption method: we assume a particular instance (D) that is both the subject description (B) and the predicate description (C). From the third figure syllogism, we deduce that some B is C. This is the converse. Then the contradictory of the converse is taken and shown to contradict the original. For the perpetual and universal propositions, they convert as temporally conditioned absolute propositions.

Key Terms
الْوُجُودِيَّتَانِ
الْخُلْفُ
الِافْتِرَاضِ
الثَّالِثِ
الْعَكْسِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Reductio and assumption
C) Contraposition
D) Induction

Key Takeaway
Affirmative propositions of certain types convert by reductio and assumption, using a third-figure syllogism and contradiction.

Conversion Rules for Particular Negatives and Special Propositions
Author explains conversion rules for particular negatives and the two special propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which particular negative propositions can be validly converted and explain the reasoning based on mutual exclusivity.

Core Idea
Only special propositions among particular negatives convert; they convert as themselves due to mutual exclusivity.

In immediate inference, conversion is a key operation, but not all propositions convert the same way. This passage zooms in on particular negatives and a special class called 'al-khāṣṣatān.' You will learn the precise rule and the logical reasoning that justifies it, as well as the method used for positives.

Guided Path
1
Locate the core claim
In the Arabic, find the sentence 'وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَّا الْخَاصَّتَانِ'. This states that among particular negatives, only the two special propositions convert.
2
Analyze the reasoning for the special propositions
Read the justification starting with 'لِأَنَّ الْوَصْفَيْنِ مُتَنَافِيَانِ فِي ذَاتٍ وَاحِدَةٍ...'. Understand that the subject and predicate are mutually exclusive under the original condition, so the conversion holds as itself.
3
Identify the method for positives
Note the list of positive propositions (existential, temporal, general absolute) that convert using 'بِالْخُلْفِ وَالِافْتِرَاضِ' (by contradiction and supposition). See how the author uses variables ب, ج, د to illustrate the third-figure reasoning.
4
Distinguish special propositions from perpetuals
The passage ends by emphasizing that the two special propositions are temporal but not perpetual. Contrast this with perpetuals, which convert as absolute temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَّا الْخَاصَّتَانِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُمَا تَنْعَكِسَانِ كَنَفْسِهِمَا ؛ لِأَنَّ الْوَصْفَيْنِ مُتَنَافِيَانِ فِي ذَاتٍ وَاحِدَةٍ بِحُكْم الْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ، وَقَدْ اجْتَمَعَا فِيهَا بِحُكْمِ الْجُزْءِ الثَّانِي، فَتِلْكَ الذَّاتُ كَمَا لَمْ تَكُنْ (ب) مَا دَامَ (ج) لَا تَكُونُ (ج) مَا دَامَ (ب) ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ . وَمِنَ الْمُوجَبَاتِ تَنْعَكِسُ الْوُجُودِيَّتَانِ وَالْوَقْتِيَّتَانِ وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ مُطْلَقَةٌ عَامَّةً ، بِالْخُلْفِ وَالِافْتِرَاضِ ، وَهُوَ أَنْ نَفْرِضَ ذَاتَ الْمَوْضُوعِ شَيْئًا وَيُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهِ وَصْفُ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَصْفُ الْمَحْمُولِ ، فَتَقُولُ: نَفْرِضُ (ج) الَّذِي هُوَ (ب) (د) ، ف(د) (ب)، و(د) (ج) ، فَبَعْضُ (ب) (ج) بِالْفِعْلِ مِنَ الثَّالِثِ، وَالْعَكْسِ، وَهُوَ أَنْ يَنْعَكِسَ نَقِيضُ الْعَكْسِ لِيَرْتَدَّ إِلَى مَا يُنَافِي الْأَصْلَ. وَالدَّائِمَتَانِ وَالْعَامَّتَانِ حِينِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ ، بِالْوُجُوهِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ. وَالْخَاصَّتَانِ حِينِيَّةً لَا دَائِمَةٌ ، أَمَّا الْحِينِيَّةُ فَلِأَنَّ لَازِمَ الْعَامِّ لَازِمُ الْخَاص ، وَأَمَّا اللَّادَوَامُ فَلَوْلَاهُ لَدَامَ الْعِنْوَانُ ، فَدَامَ الْمَحْمُولُ ، وَقَدْ فُرِضَ لَا دَائِما .
And from the particular negatives, nothing converts except the two special propositions; for they convert as themselves, because the two descriptions are mutually exclusive in one essence by the ruling of the first part, and they have come together in it by the ruling of the second part, so that essence, just as it was not (B) as long as (C), is not (C) as long as (B), and that is what is sought. And from the positives, the existential two and the temporal two convert, and the general absolute converts as a general absolute, by contradiction and supposition, which is that we suppose the essence of the subject to be something and the description of the subject and the description of the predicate are predicated on it, so you say: we suppose (C) which is (B) to be (D), so (D) is (B), and (D) is (C), so some (B) is (C) actually from the third, and the conversion, which is that the contradictory of the conversion converts to return to what contradicts the original. And the two perpetual and the two general are absolute temporal, by the mentioned aspects. And the two special are temporal, not perpetual. As for the temporality, because the necessary of the general is necessary of the particular, and as for non-perpetuity, otherwise the title would be perpetual, then the predicate would be perpetual, but it was assumed not perpetual.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rules for particular propositions. The author states that among particular negatives, only the two special propositions (al-khassatan) convert, and they convert as themselves. The reason given is that the two descriptions (the subject and predicate) are mutually exclusive in one essence by the first part (original proposition), and they come together in that essence by the second part (the conversion). Therefore, if the subject is not the predicate as long as something else, the reverse holds. For the positives, the author lists four types that convert: existential (wujūdiyyatān), temporal (waqtiyyatān), and the absolute general (mutlaqa ‘āmma) converting as absolute general. The method is 'by contradiction and supposition' (bil-khalf wal-iftirāḍ), which involves supposing an individual that is the subject and has both descriptions, leading to a particular affirmative from the third figure. Then the rule for perpetuals (dā’imatān) and generals (‘āmmatān) is that they convert as absolute temporal (hīniyya mutlaqa). Finally, the two special propositions are temporal but not perpetual: their temporality is because the necessary of the general is necessary of the particular, and non-perpetuity is argued by the fact that if they were perpetual, the title would be perpetual, leading to perpetual predicate, contrary to assumption.

Key Terms
خَاصَّتَان
عَكْس
حِينِيَّة
دَائِمَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustaqīm)
B) By contradiction and supposition (bil-khalf wal-iftirāḍ)
C) By conversion of the contradictory (ʿaks al-naqīḍ)
D) By induction (al-istiqrāʾ)

Key Takeaway
Only the two special propositions (khāṣṣatān) among particular negatives can be converted, and they convert as themselves due to the mutual exclusivity of the terms under the temporal condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Conversion by Contradiction: ʿAks al-Naqīḍ Defined
Author defines ʿaks al-naqīḍ and explains the difference between early and late definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the early and late definitions of ʿaks al-naqīḍ and apply the early definition to validly convert categorical propositions.

Core Idea
ʿAks al-naqīḍ preserves truth and quality by swapping contradictories, not just terms.

In this lesson, we examine ʿaks al-naqīḍ (conversion by contradiction), an immediate inference that differs from simple conversion. The passage presents two definitions, one from early logicians and another from later ones. Understanding which definition is used in the sciences is crucial for correctly applying this operation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate in the Arabic where the early and late definitions of ʿaks al-naqīḍ are stated. The early definition begins with 'تبديل' and the late definition with 'عند المتأخرين'.
2
Compare the changes
Note what is preserved and what changes in each definition. The early definition preserves truth and quality; the late definition preserves truth but changes quality.
3
Determine the authoritative definition
The author says 'والمعْتَبر في العلوم هو الأول' – what is considered in the sciences is the first (early) definition. This is the one to use.
4
Apply the early definition to a sample proposition
Take a proposition like 'Every prohibition is harmful.' Apply the early definition: replace subject and predicate with their contradictories, preserving quality. Result: 'Every non-harmful thing is not a prohibition.' Check that truth is preserved.
5
Analyze the rule about quality
The author states that the ruling for positives here is like that for negatives in direct conversion. This means the valid forms mirror direct conversion but with contradictories. Verify with examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَكْسُ النَّقِيض : تَبْدِيلُ نَقِيضَي الطَّرَفَيْنِ، مَعَ بَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ وَالْكَيْفِ، وَعِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخْرِينَ: جَعْلُ نَقِيض الثَّانِي أَوَّلاً وَعَيْنِ الْأَوَّلِ ثَانِيَا، مَعَ مُخَالَفَةِ الْكَيْفِ وَمُحَافَظَةِ الصِّدْقِ، وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْعُلُوم هُوَ الْأَوَّلُ . وَحُكْمُ الْمُوجَبَاتِ هَهُنَا حُكْمُ السَّوَالِبِ فِي الْمُسْتَقِيمِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ، وَالْبَيَانُ الْبَيَانُ .
Conversion by contradiction (ʿaks al-naqīḍ): replacing the two contradictories of the two extremes, while preserving truth and quality. And according to the later scholars: making the contradictory of the second the first, and the original first the second, while changing the quality and preserving truth. And what is considered in the sciences is the first. And the ruling of positives here is the ruling of negatives in direct conversion, and vice versa. And the explanation is the explanation.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage defines ʿaks al-naqīḍ (conversion by contradiction), which is an immediate inference. The author gives two definitions. The first, attributed to earlier scholars, is: swapping both subject and predicate with their contradictories while keeping truth and quality (i.e., from 'Every A is B' we derive 'Every non-B is non-A'). The second, from later scholars, is: making the contradictory of the original predicate the new subject, and the original subject the new predicate, but with a change of quality (e.g., from 'Every A is B' to 'No non-B is A'). The author notes that in logic, the first definition is what is used. He then adds a rule: for this type of conversion, the rule for positives is like that for negatives in direct conversion, and vice versa. The phrase 'the explanation is the explanation' likely means the reasoning is analogous.

Key Terms
عَكْسُ النَّقِيض
كَيْف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the definition of ʿaks al-naqīḍ according to the early scholars, what is preserved?
A) The truth and the quantity
B) The truth and the quality
C) The quantity and the quality
D) Only the truth

Key Takeaway
ʿAks al-naqīḍ is an immediate inference that replaces both terms with their contradictories while preserving truth and quality, according to the definition used in the sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Unity of Impossibility and Logical Conversion
Treating impossibility as a single absence to resolve conversion difficulty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical conversion of an impossibility proposition and explain why the author treats all impossibilities as equivalent.

Core Idea
Impossibility is a single absence, making all impossible propositions true.

The first doubt presents a challenge to conversion: a true statement about impossibility yields a false converse. The author resolves this by treating impossibility as a single absence, thereby saving the truth of the converse. This lesson unpacks that solution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its converse
The original is 'Every impossibility of the conjunction of contradictories is not a partner to the Creator' (universal negative). The converse given is 'Every partner to the Creator is a conjunction of contradictories' (universal affirmative). Note that this is not the standard conversion of a universal negative.
The author presents this as a doubt because the converse appears false.
2
Analyze the logical form
The original is of the form 'No A is B'. Its standard conversion is 'No B is A'. The converse given is 'Every B is A', which is the conversion of a universal affirmative. Why does the author use this? Because the predicate in the original is negated? Actually the original has 'not a partner', so 'A' is 'impossibility of conjunction of contradictories', 'B' is 'partner to the Creator'. The converse given is 'Every B is A' (affirmative). This is not standard; it is like an accidental conversion.
The doubt arises because the standard conversion would be 'No partner to the Creator is an impossibility...', but the author chooses a different converse to highlight a paradox.
3
Understand the solution: impossibility as a single absence
The author says 'you may commit to its truth in reality'. He then generalizes: all impossibilities are like a single absence. Just as necessity is a single existence, impossibility is a single absence. Therefore, the proposition 'Every B is A' can be considered true because both A and B are impossible, and the statement 'Every B is A' would be true if B is impossible (since it implies A is also impossible). Actually, if 'partner to the Creator' is impossible, then the universal affirmative 'Every partner to the Creator is a conjunction of contradictories' is vacuously true because the subject class is empty? But the author's move is to treat impossibility as a single absence, so the predicate 'conjunction of contradictories' is also impossible, hence the statement holds.
This is a non-standard logical move that reinterprets the semantics of impossibility.
4
Evaluate the implications for logical conversion
Under this treatment, the conversion becomes acceptable, but it changes the meaning. The standard conversion rules still hold for usual propositions, but when impossibility is involved, the author's resolution shows that we can accept the truth of the converse by equating all impossibilities. This is a special case, not a general rule.
This teaches that logical rules may need adjustment for special classes like impossibility.

وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ ، الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا كُلُّ لَا اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ لَا شَرِيكُ الْبَارِي صَادِقٌ ، مَعَ أَنَّ عَكْسَهُ كُلُّ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ كَاذِبٌ ، وَلَكَ أَنْ تَلْتَزِمَ صِدْقَهُ حَقِيقَةً ، فَافْهَمْ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا أَمْكَنَ لَكَ الْتِزَامُ تَصَادِقِ الْمُمْتَنِعَاتِ كُلِّهَا ، فَكَأَنَّ الِامْتِنَاعَ عَدَمٌ وَاحِدٌ، كَمَا أَنَّ الْوُجُوبَ وُجُودٌ وَاحِدٌ، وَيَتَأَكَّدُ التَّجْوِيزُ فِي اسْتِلْزَامِ الْمُحَالِ مُحَالًا مُطْلَقًا. وَالثَّانِي: وَلْتُمَهَّدْ مُقَدِّمَةٌ ، وَهِيَ كُلُّ مَا لَمْ يَسْتَلْزِمْ وُجُودُهُ رَفْعَ عَدَمٍ وَاقِعِيٍّ كَانَ مَوْجُودًا دَائِمًا، وَإِلَّا اسْتَلْزَمَ وُجُودُهُ رَفْعَ ذَلِكَ الْعَدَمِ ، فَنَقُولُ : قَوْلُنَا كُلَّمَا وُجِدَ الْحَادِثُ اسْتَلْزَمَ وُجُودُهُ رَفْعَ عَدَمٍ فِي الْوَاقِعِ حَقٌّ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِهَذَا الْعَكْسِ إِلَى مَا يُنَافِي الْمُقَدِّمَةَ الْمُمَهَّدَةَ. وَحَلُّهُ: مَنْعُ الْمُنَافَاةِ بَيْنَ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ اللُّزُومِيَّتَيْنِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ تَالِيهِمَا نَقِيضَيْنِ ، وَهَذِهِ شُبْهَةُ الِاسْتِلْوَامِ، وَلَهَا تَقْرِيرَاتٌ أُخَرُ مُزِلَّةٌ الْأَقْدَامِ.
And here is a doubt from two aspects. The first: that our statement 'Every impossibility of the conjunction of contradictories is not a partner to the Creator' is true, while its converse 'Every partner to the Creator is a conjunction of contradictories' is false. But you may commit to its truth in reality, so understand. And from here it becomes possible for you to commit to the truth of all impossibilities, as if impossibility is a single absence, just as necessity is a single existence. And the possibility (of implication) is strengthened in that the implication of an impossibility implies an absolute impossibility. The second: let a premise be established, which is: everything whose existence does not necessitate the removal of an actual absence exists perpetually; otherwise its existence necessitates the removal of that absence. So we say: our statement 'Whenever the contingent exists, its existence necessitates the removal of an actual absence' is true, and it converts by this conversion to something that contradicts the established premise. Its solution: deny the contradiction between the two necessary positive statements, even if their consequents are contradictories. And this is the sophism of implication, and it has other slippery formulations.

Commentary
This passage discusses two doubts (shakk) related to logical conversion ('aks) and the nature of impossibility. The first doubt concerns conversion: a statement about the impossibility of the conjunction of contradictories being predicated of 'not a partner to the Creator' is true, but its converse—'every partner to the Creator is a conjunction of contradictories'—appears false. However, the author suggests one may accept its truth in reality, because if something is impossible (like a partner to the Creator), then all impossibilities are equivalent in impossibility. This leads to the idea that all impossibilities are true in a sense, likening impossibility to a single absence, just as necessity is a single presence. The second doubt involves a premise: anything whose existence does not remove an actual absence would exist always; otherwise it would remove that absence. Applying this to the contingent (al-hadith), we say its existence removes an actual absence, but the conversion of this claim leads to a contradiction with the premise. The solution is to deny contradiction between two necessary positive propositions even if their consequents are contradictories. This is identified as the sophism of implication (shubhat al-istilzam), with other slippery formulations.

The Sophism of Implication and Its Resolution
Resolving the sophism of implication through denial of contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sophism of implication and apply the solution of denying contradiction between necessary positive statements.

Core Idea
The sophism of implication arises when necessary implication is mistaken for a contradiction between consequents.

The second doubt involves a premise and a statement about the contingent whose conversion seems to contradict the premise. The author identifies this as the sophism of implication and resolves it by denying contradiction between necessary positive propositions.

Guided Path
1
Establish the given premise and the statement about the contingent
The premise: 'Everything whose existence does not necessitate the removal of an actual absence exists perpetually; otherwise its existence necessitates the removal of that absence.' The statement: 'Whenever the contingent exists, its existence necessitates the removal of an actual absence.'
The statement about the contingent is claimed to be true.
2
Perform the conversion
Convert the statement about the contingent. The author says it converts to something that contradicts the established premise. Implicitly, the conversion yields: 'Something whose existence necessitates the removal of an actual absence exists perpetually'? Actually, the statement is of the form: if contingent exists then removal of absence. The conversion might be: 'If removal of absence, then contingent exists'? But the author says it contradicts the premise. Let's deduce: The premise says if existence does not necessitate removal, then it exists always. Contrapositive: if it does not exist always, then its existence necessitates removal. The statement about the contingent says its existence necessitates removal. But the premise implies that if existence necessitates removal, then it does not exist always? Actually, the premise: (existence does not necessitate removal) → exists always. The contrapositive: (does not exist always) → existence necessitates removal. So the statement about the contingent could be a contrapositive of the premise if the contingent does not exist always. That would not contradict. The author says the conversion leads to contradiction. So we must follow his logic exactly: the original statement 'Whenever the contingent exists, its existence necessitates removal' converts to 'Whatever has existence that necessitates removal is the contingent'? Or something. The exact conversion is unclear, but the point is that the conversion yields a proposition that contradicts the premise.
The precise conversion is not our focus; the logical structure is what matters.
3
Identify the apparent contradiction
The contradiction appears because the converted statement seems to imply that the contingent exists always or that the premise is false. The author says the contradiction is only apparent.
This is the key: it is a sophism.
4
Apply the solution: deny contradiction between necessary positives
The author says: 'Deny the contradiction between the two necessary positive statements, even if their consequents are contradictories.' In other words, two necessary positive propositions (e.g., 'If p then q' and 'If p then not q') do not contradict each other because necessity is preserved. Actually, they do contradict if they are about the same subject. The author's point is that the statements are both necessary, and the contradiction between their consequents does not make the statements themselves contradictory because they are both true under necessitation? This is a subtle point: the two propositions are 'If contingent exists then removal of absence' and the converted one 'If removal of absence then contingent exists'? But the consequents are not contradictories. Better to stick to the text: the two necessary positive statements are the original statement and the converted one; their consequents are contradictories (the converted statement likely says that the contingent does not exist always, or something). The solution is to say that both can be true because they are necessary, i.e., they are not contradictory despite the contradictoriness of their consequents. This is the sophism of implication.
This solution is specific to the structure; the important thing is to recognize the fallacy.
5
Recognize the sophism of implication
This fallacy, shubhat al-istilzam, occurs when one mistakenly thinks that necessary positive statements are contradictory because their consequents are contradictories. The correct position is that such statements can both be true, as long as the implication is necessary.
The author says it has other slippery formulations, indicating it is a widespread fallacy.

Source Text

Relations Between Significations and Rebuttal of Universal Necessary Concomitant
Author on entailments of significations and rebuttal of universal necessary concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map the entailment relations between matching, inclusion, and implication, and evaluate the claim that every meaning has a necessary rational concomitant.

Core Idea
Inclusion and implication entail matching, but not every meaning has a necessary concomitant always present to the mind.

In this passage, the author explains the logical relationships between the three types of signification: matching (muṭābaqah), inclusion (taḍammun), and implication (iltizām). He then addresses a claim by the Imam that every meaning has a rational necessary concomitant, such as 'not other than itself.' The author rebuts this by noting that such a concomitant is not always what comes to mind. Understanding these relations is crucial for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three significations
Recall that matching is when a word signifies its entire meaning; inclusion is when it signifies part of its meaning; implication is when it signifies something external associated with the meaning.
These are standard in classical logic.
2
Understand the entailment directions
The author states that both inclusion and implication entail matching. This means if a word is used in inclusion or implication, it must also have matching signification for that particular meaning.
On some views, this is apparent; on others, it requires estimative necessity.
3
Compare the views of scholars
According to al-mizāniyyān and some eminent scholars, the entailment is straightforward. However, the author (al-muṣannif) holds that implication is metaphorical and cannot coexist with literal use, so the entailment is only estimative (taqdīrī).
Estimative necessity means: 'if the word were used for that meaning, it would be matching.'
4
Analyze the converse: matching does not entail inclusion or implication
Matching does not entail inclusion because some words have simple meanings with no parts. It does not entail implication on the author's view, because every meaning has an external concomitant; but if implication were always entailed, then the word would always have an extra meaning, which is not the case.
The author's view on implication as metaphorical complicates this.
5
Examine the claim of universal necessary concomitant and its rebuttal
The Imam claimed that every meaning has at least one rational necessary concomitant, e.g., 'not other than itself.' The author rebuts: such a concomitant is not always what comes to the mind, so it does not qualify as a necessary concomitant that is always present.
This rebuttal questions whether such abstract properties are truly necessary concomitants in the logical sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ الدلالات الثلاث بعضها مع بعض: وَيَلْزَمُهُمَا الْمُطَابقة أي يلزم التضمنَ والالتزام المطابقة، وهذا بيان نسب ظاهر على رأي الميزانيين وبعض الأجلة، وغير ظاهر على رأي المصنف؛ إذ الالتزام عنده الدلالة على الخارج المستعمل فيه اللفظ مجازا، ولا يصح استعمال لفظ واحد حقيقة ومجازا معا، فلا يوجد دلالته على ما وضع له، إلا أن يقال: اللزوم تقديري بمعنى أن ههنا معنى لو استعمل فيه اللفظ يكون دلالته عليه مطابقة، ولا عكس، أي ولا يلزمها التضمن؛ لوجود ألفاظ وضعت لمعان بسيطة، والالتزام، وهو مشكل إن أريد اللزوم التقديري على رأي المصنف؛ إذ ما من معنى إلا وله خارج، لو استعمل فيه اللفظ كان دلالته عليه التزاما، وأما على رأي الميزانيين وبعض الأجلة فظاهر؛ لجواز أن يوضع لا لفظ بإزاء معنى ولما زعم الإمام أنه ما من معنى إلا وله لازم عقلي، وأقله ليس غيره دفعه بقوله : (وَكَوْنُهُ) لَيْسَ غَيْرَهُ لَيْس ممَّا يسبق إليه الذهن دائما ) . فلا يلزم من
Chapter on words: the three significations in relation to each other. Matching is entailed by both [inclusion and implication]: i.e., inclusion and implication entail matching. This explanation of the relations is apparent according to the view of the two logicians (al-mizaniyyan) and some eminent scholars, but not apparent according to the view of the author (al-musannif). For in his view, implication is signification of the external [thing] in which the word is used metaphorically, and it is not valid to use one word literally and metaphorically simultaneously, so its signification of what it was coined for does not exist. However, it could be said that the necessity is estimative (taqdiri), meaning that there is a meaning which, if the word were used for it, its signification would be matching. And not vice versa: i.e., matching does not entail inclusion; because there are words coined for simple meanings. And [it does not entail] implication; this is problematic if estimative necessity is intended according to the author's view, because there is no meaning that does not have an external [thing]; if the word were used for it, its signification would be implication. As for the view of the two logicians and some eminent scholars, it is apparent; because it is possible that no word is coined for a meaning. And when the Imam claimed that there is no meaning that does not have a rational necessary concomitant, and the least of that is 'not other than itself', he rebutted this by his saying: 'And its being not other than itself is not something that always comes to mind.' So it is not necessary from...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage, beginning with a chapter heading, discusses the logical relations between the three types of signification. The author states that inclusion and implication both entail matching (mutabaqah). This means that if a word signifies by inclusion or implication, it necessarily also signifies by matching. However, this is only apparent on the view of the two scales (al-mizaniyyan) and some great scholars. On the view of the author (al-musannif, i.e., the author of the matn), implication is signification of the external thing used metaphorically, and one cannot use a word literally and metaphorically at the same time. Therefore, for a word used metaphorically, it does not have matching signification for that metaphorical meaning. However, if we consider estimative necessity (tala'um taqdiri), meaning that if the word were used for that meaning, it would be matching. Then the author discusses the converse: matching does not entail inclusion because some words signify simple concepts that have no parts. And matching does not entail implication on the author's view, because every concept has an external concomitant; but the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) claimed that every meaning has at least one necessary concomitant, e.g., its being not other than itself. The author rebuts this: that 'not other than itself' is not always something that comes to mind (does not always present itself to the intellect). Thus, it is not a necessary concomitant that is always known. The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
اللزوم التقديري
اللازم العقلي
الميزانيان

Checkpoint

A) Because inclusion is always present.
B) Because there are words with simple meanings that have no parts.
C) Because matching is the primary type.
D) Because inclusion is rational.

Key Takeaway
While inclusion and implication entail matching, matching does not entail them, and not every meaning has a concomitant that is always present to the mind.

Correcting the Definition of Naqiḍ (Contradictory)
Critique and correction of the definition of naqiḍ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct definition of naqiḍ (contradictory) and apply the rule that contradictories of equal terms are equal.

Core Idea
The contradictory is the refutation of a thing, not the thing's refutation.

This passage clarifies a common confusion in defining the term 'naqiḍ' (contradictory). Many earlier definitions stated that 'the contradictory of every thing is its refutation (raf').' However, this leads to a logical flaw: if you apply the same wording to 'refutation' itself, you get a circular mess. The author, citing al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq, corrects the definition to 'the refutation of every thing is its contradictory.' This reversal ensures that each proposition has exactly one contradictory that negates it. The passage then extends this to a useful rule: if two terms are equal in extension, their contradictories are also equal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the flawed definition
Find the initial definition that the author criticizes: 'the contradictory of every thing is its refutation.' Understand that this phrasing makes 'raf' the contradictory of the thing.
2
Understand the critique
The problem: if we say 'the contradictory of every thing is its refutation,' then the contradictory of 'refutation' would be its own refutation, i.e., affirmation, leading to confusion. This shows the definition is ill-formed.
3
State the corrected definition
The correct definition, as given by al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq, is: 'The refutation of every thing is its contradictory.' This means that for any proposition P, its contradictory is the proposition that denies P (i.e., not-P).
In logical terms: the contradictory of P is ~P.
4
Apply the rule for equal terms
If two terms A and B are equal (i.e., have the same extension), then their contradictories (not-A and not-B) are also equal. This follows because if A and B are always both true or both false, then denying one is equivalent to denying the other.
Otherwise, they would differ in truth value, which is impossible for contradictories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ نَقِيض كُلِّ شَيْءٍ رَفْعُهُ)، وهذا فاسد؛ لما سيحقق من أن نقيض الرفع مرفوعه والصواب في العبارة ما قال السيد المحقق قدس سره: رفع كل شيء نقيضه (۲)، (فَنَقِيضَا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَتَفَارَقَا في الصدق (٤)) ، فيصدق أحدهما بدون الآخر، فيصدق مع عين الآخر؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين فلا يصدق عين ذلك النقيض ؛ لاستحالة اجتماع
Know that the contradictory of every thing is its refutation. And this is incorrect, because it will be verified that the contradictory of refutation is its affirmed. The correct expression is what al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq (may God sanctify his secret) said: The refutation of every thing is its contradictory. So the contradictories of two equal terms are equal, otherwise they differ in truth, such that one is true without the other, so it is true together with the other; because the mutual elimination of two contradictories is impossible, so the very same contradictory cannot be true; because their combination is impossible.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This short passage corrects the definition of 'contradictory' (naqiḍ). The common phrase 'the contradictory of every thing is its refutation' is flawed because then the contradictory of 'refutation' would be its 'affirmed', leading to confusion. The proper definition, attributed to al-Sayyid al-Muḥaqqiq, is 'the refutation of every thing is its contradictory'. This means that for any proposition, its contradictory is the proposition that negates it. The passage then states the rule: if two terms are equal (mutasāwiyān), their contradictories are also equal. If not, they differ in truth. It explains that if one contradictory is true, the other is false, and they cannot both be true or both false. This is the law of non-contradiction and excluded middle.

The contradictory of a proposition is its exact negation, not just any denial; and if two terms are equal, their contradictories are also equal.

The Doubt about Contradictories of Equal Concepts
Author raises a strong doubt and provides a nuanced response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which the contradictories of equal concepts are also equal, and evaluate the flaw in a common objection.

Core Idea
Not all equal concepts yield equal contradictories; the rule depends on positivity.

This passage examines a subtle logical problem: if two concepts are equal in extension, does it follow that their contradictories are also equal? The author presents a powerful doubt against this principle and then offers a nuanced solution, revealing that the rule only holds for positive concepts. Understanding this distinction prevents overgeneralization in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle under doubt
The principle claims that if two concepts are equal (e.g., 'insan' and 'naatiq' in some contexts), then their contradictories are also equal. This is what the doubt attacks.
2
Analyze the doubt's core argument
The doubt argues that the contradictory of mutual truth is simply the negation of that mutual truth, not the truth of divergence (that one is true and the other false). Moreover, the contradictory of equal concepts might have no individual instance in reality, like universal negations such as 'no partner to the Creator'.
This shows the entailment is not automatically valid.
3
Examine the initial response
The response tries to save the principle by assuming that if one contradictory is true of something and the other is not, then the exact equivalent of the first would be true, leading to a contradiction. This is argued by referring to 'two non-primeval contracts'.
This response has a flaw.
4
Spot the flaw in the response
The author notes that the truth of one contradictory might be impossible, and from an impossibility, an impossibility may follow. So the response fails because it assumes the initial truth is possible.
Thus the entailment does not hold in all cases.
5
Understand the final resolution
The rule only holds if the equal concepts are positive (like 'thing' and 'possible'), so their contradictories are negative. If the concepts are negative (like 'no partner to the Creator'), the rule does not apply because their contradictories are positive.
This condition is essential for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

صِدْقُ التَّفَارُقِ، وَرُبَّمَا يَكُونُ نَقِيضُ الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مِمَّا لَا فَرْدَ لَهُ أَصْلاً في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، كَنَقَائِضِ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ كُلَّ مَفْهُومٍ، فَيَصْدُقُ الْأَوَّلُ، أَيْ: عَدَمُ التَّصَادُقِ، دُونَ الثَّانِي، أَيِ: التَّفَارُقِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ عَدَمُ صِدْقِ أَحَدِهِمَا مَعَ صِدْقِ الْآخَرِ. وَقَدْ يُجَابُ بِأَنَّ مَرْجِعَ التَّسَاوِي إِلَى عَقْدَيْنِ غَيْرِ وَأَيْضاً يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَصْدُقَ عَيْنُ النَّقِيضِ الْمَفْرُوضِ الصِّدْقِ عَلَى شَيْءٍ عَلَيْهِ مَعَهُ، وَلَمْ يَصْدُقِ الْآخَرُ عَلَيْهِ، بَلْ عَيْنُهُ لِذَلِكَ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ أَحَدِ الَّتِي لَا تَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ رَفْعُ التَّصَادِقِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ التَّفَارُقَ، فَبَعْدَ تَسْلِيمِهِ، فِيهِ إِشَارَةٌ إِلَى مَا سَيَجِيءُ فِي تَحْقِيقِ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ، مِنْ فِيهِ؛ فَإِنَّهُمَا رَفْعَانِ، فَنَقِيضَاهُمَا الْمَرْفُوعَانِ الْوُجُودِيَّانِ، عَلَى مَا سَيُحَقِّقُ الْمُصَنِّفُ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ، فَلَا يَرِدُ مَا قَالَ بَعْضُ الشُّرَّاحِ: إِنَّ نَقِيضَ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ رَفْعُهُ، فَنَقِيضَا الْمَفْهُومَيْنِ السَّلْبِيَّيْنِ أَيْضاً رَفْعُهُمَا. فَلَا جَوَابَ لَا بِتَخْصِيصِ الدَّعْوَى بِغَيْرِ نَقَائِضِ تِلْكَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الشَّامِلَةِ.
Chapter on Simple Meanings. [Regarding] the two contradictories, (then the truth of one of the two equals without the other would follow, this is a contradiction.) Here is a strong doubt, which is: that the entailment is denied; because the contradictory of mutual truth is its negation, not the truth of divergence, and perhaps the contradictory of the two equals is something that has no individual at all in reality, like the contradictories of concepts that encompass every concept, so the first is true, i.e., the absence of mutual truth, without the second, i.e., divergence; because it is the non-truth of one of them along with the truth of the other. And it may be answered that the reference of equality is to two non-primeval contracts, so we say: if the truth of one contradictory of the equals on a thing is assumed, and the other contradictory is not true, then its exact equivalent is true, to the end of the proof. But there is reflection in it; because it is possible that the truth of one of the two contradictories on a thing is impossible, so it is possible that the other contradictory nor its exact equivalent is true on that supposition; because the impossible may entail the impossible. Also, it is possible that the exact equivalent of the assumed-true contradictory is true on a thing along with it, and the other is not true on it, but rather its exact equivalent for that reason, so the truth of one of the two equals without the other does not follow. And what was said: that the truth of a negation on a thing does not require its existence; for its truth does not entail except the truth of the affirmative with negative predicate, which does not require the existence of the subject. And then the negation of mutual truth entails divergence, so after conceding it, there is an indication to what will come in the investigation of categorical propositions, that the affirmative with negative predicate and the modified are both equal in requiring the existence of the subject. It only holds if those equal concepts are taken as positive, like 'thing' and 'possible', so that their contradictories are negative. But if they are negative, like 'no partner to the Creator' and 'no conjunction of two contradictories', then there is no room for that; for they are two negations, so their contradictories are the positive negations, as the author will verify. So it does not refute what some commentators said: that the contradictory of everything is its negation, so the contradictories of two negative concepts are also their negations. There is no answer except by specifying the claim to other than the contradictories of those encompassing concepts.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of a logical rule concerning the contradictories of equal (mutasawiyan) concepts. The author presents a strong doubt (shakk qawiyy) against the claim that if two concepts are equal, then their contradictories are also equal, and then provides a detailed refutation. The doubt argues that the entailment (mulazama) is denied because the contradictory of mutual truth (tasadiq) is simply its negation (raf'uhu), not the truth of divergence (sidq al-tafarruq). Moreover, the contradictory of the equals might have no instance in reality, like universal negations. The response (jawab) is that the reference of equality is to two non-primeval contracts, and then if one contradictory is true of something and the other is not, the exact equivalent of the first would be true, leading to a contradiction. However, the author notes a flaw (nazar) in this response: it assumes that the truth of one contradictory is possible, but if it is impossible, the entailment fails. The discussion then shifts to the nature of negative statements (salbiyya), clarifying that the truth of a negative does not imply the existence of the subject, and that the rule about contradictories of equals only holds if the concepts are positive (wujudiyya), like 'thing' and 'possible'. For negative concepts, like 'no partner to the Creator', their contradictories are positive and the rule does not apply. This careful analysis reveals the precise conditions under which the rule holds, guarding against overgeneralization.
