The Rule of Contradictories of General and Specific Terms
Author states the rule and presents two doubts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of contradictories of general and specific terms and identify counterexamples that challenge its universality.

Core Idea
The contradictory of the general is more specific, and vice versa—but not always.

In this passage, the author presents a key rule in logical analysis: the inversion of generality and specificity when taking contradictories. He provides a logical proof for why negating the general entails negating the specific, but not vice versa. He then raises two doubts that suggest the rule is not absolute, using examples from negative concepts and modal terms. Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to reason about definitions and their negations in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Read the first sentence: 'wa naqid al-a'am wa al-akhass mutlaqan bil-'aks'. The rule says the relation between contradictories is the inverse: the contradictory of the general is more specific, and the contradictory of the specific is more general.
2
Walk through the proof
The author argues: if A is more general than B, then the negation of A (not-A) entails the negation of B (not-B). For example, 'animal' is more general than 'human'. Not-animal includes everything that is not an animal, which is a subset of not-human, so not-animal is more specific.
This works because B is included in A, so what is excluded from A is also excluded from B.
3
Check the converse
The author says there is no converse: not-B does not entail not-A. Using the example, not-human (e.g., a dog) does not imply not-animal (since a dog is an animal). So the negation of the specific does not necessitate the negation of the general.
This is because the general may apply to things outside the specific.
4
Examine the first doubt
The author raises a doubt: the concept 'no conjunction of two contradictories' (a negative concept) is more general than 'human'. But their contradictories are divergent, not related as general-specific. This shows the rule may fail when the general term is a negative concept.
The contradictory of the negative concept is 'conjunction of two contradictories' (impossible), while the contradictory of 'human' is everything non-human. These are not in a general-specific relation.
5
Consider the second doubt
The author mentions 'general possibility' (al-mumkin al-amm) and 'specific possibility' (al-mumkin al-khass). The passage cuts off, but the implication is that the rule may also fail for modal terms where the relation is not straightforward inclusion.
This doubt hints that the rule requires refinement when dealing with negative or modal concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا، أي خذه وأتقنه، ونقيض الأعم والأخص مطلقا بالعكس، أي النسبة بين نقيض الأعم أخص، ونقيض الأخص أعم؛ فإن انتفاء العام ملزوم لانتفاء الخاص، وإلا وجد الخاص بدون العام، فكلما صدق عليه رفع العام صدق عليه رفع الخاص، وأما الثاني فلأنه لا عكس، أي ليس ارتفاع الخاص مستلزما لارتفاع العام؛ إذ قد يصدق العام في مباين الخاص؛ تحقيقا لمعنى العموم. وشكك بأن لا اجتماع النقيضين أعم من الإنسان، مع أن بين نقيضيهما تباينا كليا، والأولى ليس عموما وخصوصا، وأيضا، إشارة إلى الشك الثاني أن الممكن العام أعم من الممكن الخاص، فنقيضاهما
This (take it and master it): And the contradictory of the more general and the more specific, absolutely, is inverse: i.e., the relation between the contradictory of the more general is more specific, and the contradictory of the more specific is more general. For the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific, otherwise the specific would exist without the general. So whenever the negation of the general is true of something, the negation of the specific is true of it. And as for the second, because there is no converse: i.e., the negation of the specific does not entail the negation of the general, since the general may be true in something distinct from the specific; this is a realization of the meaning of generality. And a doubt was raised: that 'no conjunction of two contradictories' is more general than 'human', even though between their contradictories there is absolute divergence, and the former is not a relation of generality and particularity. And also, an indication to the second doubt: that general possibility is more general than specific possibility, so their contradictories
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines the rule (qa'ida) about the relation between the contradictories of general (a'am) and specific (akhass) terms. The author states that the contradictory of the general is more specific, and the contradictory of the specific is more general. This is proven by the entailment: if the general is negated, the specific must also be negated, because the specific is included under the general. However, the converse does not hold: negating the specific does not entail negating the general, because the general may apply to things outside the specific. The author then presents two doubts (shukuk) against this rule. The first doubt: the concept 'no conjunction of two contradictories' (la ijtimaa' al-naqidayn) is more general than 'human' (insan), yet their contradictories are divergent (mutabayin), not related as general and specific. This challenges the rule. The second doubt: 'general possibility' (al-mumkin al-amm) is more general than 'specific possibility' (al-mumkin al-khass), and their contradictories would then need to be examined. The passage cuts off, but the implied critique questions whether the rule holds universally. This careful analysis shows that the rule is not absolute and must be applied with attention to the nature of the terms.

The Rule of Negations of General and Specific Terms
Commentary explains the rule of negations and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general, and identify cases where the rule fails due to inclusive concepts.

Core Idea
The negation of the general is specific, but the negation of the specific is not necessarily general.

This lesson unpacks a subtle rule about how negations behave when terms differ in generality. Understanding this rule is crucial for valid immediate inferences in Islamic logic, especially when moving from the negation of a broader category to a narrower one, or vice versa. The commentary also addresses important objections that clarify the scope of the rule.

Guided Path
1
State the rule in your own words
Write out the rule as given: The negation of the more general term is more specific, and the negation of the more specific term is more general. Ensure you understand the direction: Naqīḍ al-aʿamm akhaṣṣ, wa naqīḍ al-akhass aʿamm.
2
Analyze the proof for the first part
The proof says: if the general is removed (negated), the specific must also be removed, because the specific implies the general. So the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific, making it more specific. Confirm this with an example: if 'animal' is negated, 'human' is also negated.
The proof uses the logical relation of entailment between concepts.
3
Examine why the converse fails
The text explicitly states 'lā ʿaks': removing the specific does not necessitate removing the general, because the general applies to things beyond the specific. For example, 'not human' does not entail 'not animal' since animals other than humans exist. This is the essence of generality.
This is a key distinction: the rule is one-way.
4
Review the first objection and its resolution
The first objection: 'non-compatibility of two contradictories' (lā ijtimāʿ al-naqīḍayn) is more general than 'human', but their negations have total divergence (tabāyun kullī), not a general-particular relation. The commentator resolves by noting the rule applies only to positive concepts that actually apply to something, not to negative concepts like 'non-compatibility'.
This shows the rule is restricted to 'inclusive concepts' (al-mafhumāt al-shāmila).
5
Analyze the second objection and its resolution
The second objection: 'general possible' (al-mumkin al-ʿāmm) is more general than 'specific possible' (al-mumkin al-khāṣṣ), but their negations seem to invert the rule. The resolution: the term 'non-general possible' does not actually apply to anything, so the proposition 'every non-general possible is non-specific possible' is vacuously true, and the objection dissolves. The rule holds for terms that have instances.
This reinforces the condition that the descriptive attribute must be true for the proposition to be meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا، أي خذه وأتقنه ، وَنَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصَّ مُطْلَقًا بِالْعَكْسِ، أي النسبة بين نقيض الأعم أخص ، ونقيض الأخص أعم ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامَ مَلْزُومٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاص، وإلا وجد الخاص بدون العام، فكلما صدق عليه رفع العام صدق عليه رفع الخاص، وأما الثاني فلأنه لَا عَكْسَ، أي ليس ارتفاع الخاص مستلزما لارتفاع العام ؛ إذ قد يصدق العام في مباين الخاص؛ تَحْقِيقا لِمَعْنَى الْعُمُوم. وَشُكِّكَ بِأَنَّ لَا اجْتِمَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ، مَعَ أَنَّ بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْهِمَا تَبَايُنَا كُلَّيَّا، والأولى ليس عموما وخصوصا، وَأَيْضًا إشارة إلى الشك الثاني أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَ الْعَامَّ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنِ الْخَاصَّ، فنقيضاهما بالعكس، فَكُلُّ لَا مُمْكِن عَامَ لامُمْكِن خاص، وكُلُّ لايمكن خاص إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ؛ لأن كل لاممكن خاص إما ضروري الوجود أو ضروري العدم، فكل لاممكن عام إما واجب أو ممتنع، وكلاهما ممكن عام. انحل لَا مُمْكِن عَامَ مُمْكِنْ عَامٌ، والجواب بما مر، من تخصيص بغير نقائض المفهومات الشاملة. لا يقال: لو لم يصدق كل لاممكن عام لاممكن خاص يصدق بعض لاممكن عام ممكن خاص هذا خلف، فالشك بحاله؛ لأن اللاممكن العام لا يصدق على شيء؛ حتى يصدق عليه الممكن الخاص أو نقيضه؛ إذ صدق الوصف العنواني شرط في انعقاد القضية، وبهذا ينحل الشبهة بأن ضروري الطرفين ليس بموجود، فبعض ما ليس بموجود ضروري الطرفين. ثم نقول: كل ما ليس بموجود إما ممكن أو ممتنع، وكلاهما ليس بضروري الطرفين. فلا شيء مما ليس بموجود ضروري الطرفين، وهو متناقض للأول، وكذا انحل الشبهة بأن الذي وجوده وعدمه يستلزم المحال إما موجود أو معدوم، وعلى كلا التقديرين يلزم المحال.
This: take it and master it. And the negation of the absolute more general and more specific is opposite, meaning the ratio between the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general; for the non-existence of the general necessitates the non-existence of the specific, otherwise the specific would exist without the general, so whenever the removal of the general applies to something, the removal of the specific applies to it; and as for the second, because there is no converse, i.e. the removal of the specific does not necessitate the removal of the general, since the general may apply to something distinct from the specific, realizing the meaning of generality. And it was objected that 'non-compatibility of two contradictories' is more general than 'human', while between their negations there is total divergence, and the first is not generality and particularity; and also, indicating the second objection, that the general possible is more general than the specific possible, so their negations are opposite, so every non-general possible is non-specific possible, and every non-specific possible is either necessary or impossible; because every non-specific possible is either necessary of existence or necessary of non-existence, so every non-general possible is either necessary or impossible, and both are general possible. It is resolved: non-general possible is general possible. And the answer is what passed, by specifying the claim for other than the negations of inclusive concepts. It cannot be said: if not every non-general possible is non-specific possible, then some non-general possible is specific possible; this is a contradiction, so the doubt remains; because the non-general possible does not apply to anything, such that the specific possible or its negation applies to it; since the truth of the descriptive attribute is a condition for the constitution of the proposition. And by this the difficulty is resolved: that the necessary from both sides does not exist, so some of what does not exist is necessary from both sides. Then we say: everything that does not exist is either possible or impossible, and both are not necessary from both sides. So nothing of what does not exist is necessary from both sides, and it contradicts the first. And likewise the difficulty is resolved: that that whose existence and non-existence necessitate the impossible is either existent or non-existent, and on both suppositions the impossible follows.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains a key rule about the relationship between the negations of more general and more specific terms. It opens by stating the rule: the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general. The commentator emphasizes 'take it and master it'. The proof is given: if the general is removed, the specific must also be removed, otherwise the specific could exist without the general, which is impossible. However, the converse does not hold: removing the specific does not remove the general, because the general applies to things outside the scope of the specific—this is the essence of generality. The passage then addresses two objections. First, the concept 'non-compatibility of two contradictories' is more general than 'human', but their negations have total divergence, not a general-particular relation. Second, the 'general possible' is more general than the 'specific possible', but their negations appear to invert the rule: 'non-general possible' implies 'non-specific possible', but 'non-specific possible' includes both necessary and impossible, which are not possible in the usual sense. The commentator resolves both objections by noting that the rule applies only to terms that are positive or 'existent' in the sense of 'thing' or 'possible', not to purely negative concepts like 'non-general possible' which do not actually apply to anything. The description must actually apply for the proposition to hold. Thus, the doubt is resolved.

Key Terms
نقيض
أعم
أخص
مطلقا
المفهومات الشاملة

The rule that the negation of the more general is more specific is one-way and applies only to positive inclusive concepts, as demonstrated by the objections and resolutions in the commentary.

Negations of Terms with Partial Overlap (من وجه)
Commentary explains relationship between negations of terms with partial overlap (min wajh).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict the relationship between the negations of two terms that have partial overlap (min wajh) and distinguish it from the relationship for absolute general-specific terms.

Core Idea
Negations of partially overlapping terms diverge partially, like negations of totally different terms.

This passage, from a commentary on logic, clarifies a subtle point: when two terms are neither completely general-particular nor completely different, their negations do not follow the simple 'opposite' pattern. Instead, they exhibit partial divergence (tabayun juz'i), just like the negations of two mutually exclusive terms. Understanding this prevents logical mistakes in analyzing definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two original terms
Find the two universals that are related by partial overlap (min wajh). For example, 'human' (insan) and 'white' (abyad) share some members but not all.
Use your own example from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'salat' and 'du'a'.
2
Recall the relationship for absolute general-specific terms
For terms where one is absolutely general and the other absolutely specific, the negations follow a hierarchical pattern. But the passage contrasts with that case. Here, the negations behave like those of completely different terms.
If needed, compare with the negations of 'animal' (general) and 'human' (specific)—a different relationship.
3
Apply the rule to a min wajh pair
Take a pair like 'human' and 'white'. Their negations are 'non-human' and 'non-white'. Some non-humans are white, some non-whites are human, some are neither, and some are both? Actually, check: a white cat is non-human and white, so it is in non-human but not in non-white. A black human is non-white and human, so in non-white but not in non-human. So the negations overlap (e.g., a black cat is both non-human and non-white) and diverge (each includes instances the other excludes). Hence partial divergence.
4
Distinguish from absolute general-specific negation
Contrast with a pair like 'animal' and 'human': 'non-animal' excludes all animals, 'non-human' includes everything except humans. Non-human includes non-animal plus non-human animals (like horses). So non-animal is a subset of non-human—they are in a general-specific relationship, not partial divergence.
This distinction is crucial for accurate logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعاني المفردة. وَبَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْ الْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصَّ مِنْ وَجْه تبايِّنُ جُزْتَيَّ كَالْمُتباين). أي النسبة بين والأخص من الأعم والأخص كما بين نقيضي المتباينين تباين جزئي، كذلك بين نقيضي من وجه، (وَهُوَ التَّفَارُقُ)، أي تفارق كل واحد من الكليين عن الآخر وجه.
Section on simple meanings. And between the negations of the more general and more specific with partial overlap there is partial divergence, like the two mutually exclusive. i.e. the ratio between the more specific of the more general and the more specific is as between the negations of two mutually exclusive: partial divergence. Likewise between the two negations of (two terms related) by partial overlap, and it is the divergence, i.e. each of the two universals diverges from the other in some respect.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This short passage opens a new section on simple meanings. It explains the relationship between the negations of two terms that are not in a relation of absolute generality-particularity, but rather partial overlap (min wajh). In such a case, their negations do not follow the opposite rule found with absolute terms. Instead, they have a relationship of partial divergence (tabayun juz'i), similar to the negations of two completely different (mutabayin) terms. The commentator clarifies that partial divergence means each of the two universals differs from the other in some respect—they overlap in some individuals but not in others. For example, 'human' and 'white' overlap in some (white humans) but diverge in others (non-white humans and non-human whites). Their negations, 'non-human' and 'non-white', also have a partial overlap-divergence relationship. The passage is cut off, but it introduces the rule for such cases.

Key Terms
من وجه (min wajh): Partial overlap; two terms that intersect in some instances but not all. [Note: Standard logical terminology in post-classical Arabic logic.]
تباين جزئي (tabayun juz'i): Partial divergence; the two terms are neither identical nor completely different; they share some instances but not others. [Note: Stable term.]
متباينان (mutabayinan): Two terms that are completely different, with no overlap in extension. [Note: Standard.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The negations are partially divergent, like the negations of two completely different terms.
C) The negations are identical.
D) The negations are completely contradictory.

Key Takeaway
When two terms have partial overlap (min wajh), their negations also partially overlap and diverge, mirroring the relationship between negations of totally distinct terms.

Understanding Partial Opposition in Logical Relations
Author explains partial opposition and gives examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify pairs of terms that exhibit partial opposition and distinguish it from total opposition and general-special relation.

Core Idea
Partial opposition is about the relation of the contradictories, not the affirmatives themselves.

We have learned about total opposition and general-special relations. Now we encounter a more subtle relation: partial opposition. In this passage, the author defines it and shows how it can be embedded within other opposition types. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze term relations in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of partial opposition
Focus on the key condition: when the identical (affirmative) of one term is true together with the contradictory of the other, but not with its own contradictory.
The author writes: 'so wherever the identical of one of them is true, while the contradictory of the other is true, then each of the two contradictories is true together with the identical of the other, so it is not true with its contradictory.'
2
Compare with total opposition
In total opposition, the two affirmatives cannot be true together; in partial opposition, the affirmatives may not overlap, but their contradictories do.
Total opposition (tabayun kulli) vs partial opposition (tabayun juz'i).
3
Trace the examples within total opposition
The example 'not-stone' and 'not-animal' have general-special, but their contradictories 'stone' and 'human' have total opposition. Check why: stone and human are disjoint.
Also 'human' and 'not-rational' have total opposition.
4
Trace the examples within general-special
The pair 'white' and 'human' have general-special; their negations also have general-special. Understand the reversal: 'stone' and 'animal' have total opposition, but their negations have general-special.
This shows partial opposition can be nested within either other relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الْجُمْلَةِ إما كليا أو جزئيا ؛ لأَنَّ بَيْنَ الْعَيْنَيْنِ تَفَارُقًا، فَحَيْثُ يَصْدُقُ عَيْنَ أَحَدِهِمَا، والحال أنه يَصْدُقُ نَقِيضُ الْآخَرِ، فيصدق كل من النقيضين مع عين الآخر ، فلا يصدق مع نقيضه، وَهُوَ أي التباين الجزئي قَدْ يَتَحَقِّقُ في ضِمْنِ التَبايُنِ الْكُلِّيِّ، كَاللَّا حَجَرِ وَاللَّا حَيَوَانِ، فبينهما عموم من وجه وبين نقيضيهما وهما الحجر والإنسان تباين كلي، وَالْإِنْسَانِ وَاللَّانَاطِقِ، بينهما تباين كلي، وكذا بين نقيضيهما، وهما اللا إنسان والناطق. وَقَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي ضِمْنِ الْعُمُومِ مِنْ وَجْهِ، كَالْأَبْيَضِ وَالْإِنْسَانِ، بينهما عموم من وجه، وكذا بين نقيضيهما: اللاأبيض واللا إنسان، وَالْحَجَرِ وَالْحَيَوَانِ، بينهما تباين كلي، وبين نقيضيهما هما اللاحجر واللاحيوان عموم من وجه. وَهَهُنَا سُؤَالُ وَجَوَابٌ عَلَى طِبْقِ مَا مَرَّ. قيل في تقريره: بين الإنسان واللاشيء تباين كلي، وبين نقيضيهما وهما اللا إنسان والشيء عموم مطلق، والجواب بتخصيص الدعوى بنقائض المتباينين الذين لهما أفراد يصدقان عليهما، ولا يخفى أن السؤال لا يتأتى في نقيضي الأعم والأخص من وجه، ثم إن المتباينين قد أخذ في مفهومهما التفارق، فلا بد أن يكون لهما أفراد، فاللاشيء إن لم يكن له أفراد فليس بينه وبين الإنسان تباين، وإن كان له أفراد فلا يصدق عليه الإنسان، وكذا الشيء، بل يصدق عليه اللاإنسان بدون الشيء، فبينهما تباين جزئي، والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب.
In general it is either total or partial; because between the two individuals there is difference, so wherever the identical of one of them is true, while the contradictory of the other is true, then each of the two contradictories is true together with the identical of the other, so it is not true with its contradictory. And it, i.e., partial opposition, may occur within total opposition, like 'not-stone' and 'not-animal', for between them there is general-special from one aspect, and between their two contradictories—which are stone and human—there is total opposition. And [like] 'human' and 'not-rational', between them there is total opposition, and likewise between their two contradictories, which are 'not-human' and 'rational'. And it may occur within general-special from one aspect, like 'white' and 'human', between them there is general-special from one aspect, and likewise between their two contradictories: 'not-white' and 'not-human'. And [like] 'stone' and 'animal', between them there is total opposition, and between their two contradictories—which are 'not-stone' and 'not-animal'—there is general-special from one aspect. And here there is a question and answer on the pattern of what preceded. It was said in its formulation: between 'human' and 'not-thing' there is total opposition, and between their two contradictories—which are 'not-human' and 'thing'—there is absolute generality. And the answer is by restricting the claim to the contradictories of those two opposites that have individuals upon which they are true. And it is not hidden that the question does not apply to the contradictories of the more general and more specific from one aspect. Then, the two opposites have difference taken in their concept, so they must have individuals. So if 'not-thing' has no individuals, then there is no opposition between it and 'human'. And if it has individuals, then 'human' is not true of them, and likewise for 'thing'. Rather, 'not-human' is true without 'thing', so between them there is partial opposition. And God Almighty knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines partial opposition (at-tabāyun al-juz'ī) and explains how it can be subsumed under total opposition or under the relation of general-special from one aspect (al-‘umūm min wajh). The author begins by stating that the difference between two individuals (al-‘aynān) is either total or partial. He then describes the criterion for partial opposition: when the identical (the affirmative) of one is true together with the contradictory (negative) of the other, and vice versa, but not true together with the contradictory of the same one. This is in contrast to total opposition where the two affirmatives cannot be true together. The author gives examples: ‘not-stone’ and ‘not-animal’ have general-special from one aspect (since everything that is not-stone is not necessarily not-animal, but some are), while their contradictories ‘stone’ and ‘human’ have total opposition (no overlap). Similarly, ‘human’ and ‘not-rational’ have total opposition because every human is rational, so ‘human’ and ‘not-rational’ cannot coincide. Then he shows how partial opposition can occur within general-special from one aspect, as in ‘white’ and ‘human’: some whites are human and some are not, so the relation is general-special from one aspect; the same holds for their negations. The second example ‘stone’ and ‘animal’ illustrates that total opposition and general-special can be reversed between affirmatives and negatives. Finally, there is a question and answer: a potential counterexample using ‘human’ and ‘not-thing’ seems to show total opposition, but the answer clarifies that the claim about partial opposition under total opposition applies only to opposites that have actual individuals. ‘Not-thing’ has no individuals (since everything is a thing), so it is not a valid opposite. Thus, the argument stands. The passage uses logical terminology from the mantiq tradition and is essential for understanding the types of opposition (taqābul) in Aristotelian logic.

Key Terms
التباين الجزئي
التباين الكلي
عموم من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs exhibits partial opposition according to the passage?
A) 'Human' and 'not-rational'
B) 'White' and 'human'
C) 'Stone' and 'animal'
D) 'Not-stone' and 'not-animal'

Key Takeaway
Partial opposition is a relation between two terms where their contradictories overlap while the affirmatives do not; it can occur within total opposition or general-special, but only for terms that have actual individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the pair 'non-human' and 'not-animal', determine whether they exhibit total opposition, partial opposition, or general-special. Justify using the criteria from the passage.
?
Construct a pair of terms that exemplifies partial opposition within the relation of general-special from one aspect, and explain why it fits.

Two Definitions of Evident Concomitant
Author defines division of lazim into bayyin and ghayr bayyin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two definitions of an evident necessary concomitant (al-lazim al-bayyin) and identify which is more general.

Core Idea
Evident has two senses: one self-evident from the subject, another evident from both terms and the relation.

This passage presents a crucial division in Islamic logic: the necessary concomitant (lazim) can be either evident (bayyin) or non-evident (ghayr bayyin). But 'evident' itself has two definitions. The first is narrower: a concomitant is evident if merely conceiving the subject (malzum) gives you the concomitant. The second is broader: it is evident if conceiving both the subject and the concomitant together with the relation between them yields certainty of the concomitance. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to determine when an inference is immediate and when it requires a middle term – a skill essential for rigorous logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of 'evident' (bayyin)
In the Arabic, find the phrase that defines the first sense: 'الذي يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم' (that whose conception follows from conceiving the subject). Then find the second sense: 'الذي يلزم من تصورهما، أي تصور اللازم والملزوم مع تصور النسبة الجزم باللزوم' (that from whose conception of both – i.e., conceiving the concomitant and subject together with the relation – certainty of the concomitance follows).
The second definition explicitly adds 'conception of the relation' as a condition.
2
Note the example given
The passage gives 'al-a'zamiyya lil-kull' (being greater for the whole) as an example. Which definition does it fit? It requires conceiving both the whole and the part and the relation of 'greater than' to be certain, so it falls under the second definition.
The first definition would require that merely thinking of 'whole' yields 'greater than its part' – which may not be immediate without considering the part.
3
Determine the generality
The passage states that the second definition is more general (a'am) than the first. Why? Because the first requires the concomitant to be conceived solely from the subject, while the second only requires certainty from conceiving both terms and the relation – a weaker condition. Conversely, the non-evident in the second sense is more specific (akhass) than the evident in the second sense.
This is explained by the investigator (al-muhaqqiq): the generality appears only if we consider in the first what was considered in the second – namely the sufficiency of conception of both for certainty.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider the statement 'The Quran is the word of Allah.' Is this evident? In the first sense, conceiving 'Quran' does not immediately give 'word of Allah' (many people know the Quran without that belief). In the second sense, if you conceive both 'Quran' and 'word of Allah' and the relation of being created vs. uncreated, you may still need proof. So it is non-evident. Contrast with 'The whole is greater than its part' which is evident in the second sense.
This exercise trains you to classify propositions by their evidentness, a skill used in usul al-fiqh and kalam to determine when a proof is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَيْضًا، إشارة إلى تقسيم آخر لللازم: اللازم إما بين، وهو الذي يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم، وقد يقال البين على الذي يلزم من تصورهما، أي تصور اللازم والملزوم مع تصور النسبة الجزم باللزوم، كالأعظمية للكل، وهو بالمعنى الثاني أعم من الأول. قال المحقق العموم إنما يظهر إذا اعتبر في الأول ما اعتبر في الثاني، وهو كفاية تصورهما الجزم باللزوم، أو غير بين بخلافه، أي بخلاف البين بأحد المعنيين، فالنسبة بالعكس، الغير البين بالمعنى الثاني أخص منه.
And also, an indication of another division of the necessary concomitant: The necessary concomitant is either evident (bayyin), that is, that whose conception follows from the conception of the subject; and the evident may be defined as that from whose conception of both (i.e., conception of the concomitant and the subject together with conception of the relation) certainty of the concomitance follows, like being greater for the whole. And it (the second meaning) is more general than the first. The investigator said: Generality becomes apparent only if we consider in the first what was considered in the second, namely the sufficiency of the conception of both for certainty of the concomitance. Or it is non-evident (ghayr bayyin) contrary to it, i.e., contrary to the evident in either sense, so the relation is reversed: the non-evident in the second sense is more specific than it.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage defines the division of the necessary concomitant (lazim) into evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin). Two definitions of 'evident' are given. The first: a concomitant is evident if merely conceiving the subject (malzum) leads to conceiving the concomitant. The second: a concomitant is evident if conceiving both the subject and the concomitant together with the relation between them leads to certainty of the concomitance. An example is the relation of being greater for the whole (i.e., the whole is greater than its part). The second definition is more general (a'am) than the first because it does not require the concomitant to be conceived solely from the subject; it only requires certainty of concomitance from conceiving both terms and the relation. The non-evident is the opposite: conceiving the subject and concomitant does not produce certainty. The passage notes that the non-evident in the second sense is more specific (akhass) than the evident in the second sense. This division is important in logic for understanding immediate and mediated inferences.

D) Water is wet.

Key Takeaway
The division of the necessary concomitant into evident and non-evident depends on whether certainty of the concomitance arises from conceiving the subject alone, or from conceiving both the subject and the concomitant together with the relation.

Classification of the Necessary Concomitant: Clear vs. Non-Clear
Author defines two meanings of 'clear' concomitant and their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of 'clear concomitant' and identify examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Clear concomitants are known from conceptualization alone; non-clear ones require proof.

The logicians distinguish concomitants that are immediately known from those that require additional reasoning. This classification is crucial for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh: when you claim a property necessarily follows from something, you must know whether that following is self-evident or needs evidence. The passage defines two levels of clarity and shows how they relate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The passage divides lazim into bayyin (clear) and ghayr bayyin (non-clear). Note that bayyin has two sub-definitions.
2
Understand the first sense of bayyin
First sense: conceptualization (tasawwur) of the malzum alone yields conceptualization of the lazim. Example: 'greater' from 'whole' – once you conceive 'whole', you immediately conceive 'greater'.
This is a hadd-like definition.
3
Understand the second sense of bayyin
Second sense: conceptualization of both the malzum and the lazim together with the relation yields certainty (jazm) of the concomitance. Example: again 'greater for the whole' works, but this sense also includes cases where the first sense may not hold.
This second sense is more general.
4
Analyse the relationship of generality
The second meaning is more general because any concomitant clear in the first sense is also clear in the second, but not conversely. The commentator (al-muhaqqiq) clarifies that this generality appears only if we consider in the first what is considered in the second, i.e., sufficiency of conceptualization for certainty.
For non-clear, the relation reverses: the non-clear in the second sense is more specific than in the first sense.
5
Check real existence
Both types of clear and non-clear exist in reality. This means some concomitants are immediately known, while others require external proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللَّازِمُ إِمَّا بَيِّنٌ، وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَلْزَمْ تَصَوُّرُهُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَلْزُومِ، وَقَدْ يُقَالُ البَيِّنُ عَلَى الَّذِي يَلْزَمْ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِهِمَا، أي تصور اللازم والملزوم مع تصور النسبة الْجَزْمُ بِاللُّزوم كالأعظمية للكل، وَهُوَ بالمعنى الثاني أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ. قال المحقق العموم إنما يظهر إذا اعتبر في الأول ما اعتبر في الثاني، وهو كفاية تصورهما الجزمَ باللزوم، أَوْ غَيْرُ بَيِّنِ بِخِلَافِهِ، أي بخلاف البين بأحد المعنيين، فَالنِّسْبَةُ بِالْعَكْسِ، الغير البين بالمعنى الثاني أخص منه بالمعنى الأول؛ لأن نقيض الأخص أعم، وَكُلُّ مِنْهُمَا مَوْجُودٌ في الواقع.
The concomitant is either clear – and it is that whose conceptualization follows from the conceptualization of the thing itself – and it may be said that the clear is that from whose conceptualization (i.e., conceptualization of the concomitant and the thing together with conceptualization of the relation) certainty of the concomitance follows, like 'greater' for the whole. And it (the second meaning) is more general than the first. The chief investigator said: the generality only appears if we consider in the first what is considered in the second, namely the sufficiency of their conceptualization for certainty of the concomitance. Or it is non-clear, contrary to it (i.e., contrary to the clear in either meaning). So the relation is reversed: the non-clear in the second meaning is more specific than it in the first meaning, because the negation of the more specific is more general. And both exist in reality.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage classifies the necessary concomitant (al-lazim) into two main types: clear (bayyin) and non-clear (ghayr bayyin). The clear is further defined in two ways. First sense: a concomitant whose conceptualization (tasawwur) follows immediately from conceptualizing the thing itself (al-malzum) alone, without needing anything else. Example: the concept 'greater' follows from the concept 'whole' – no additional inference is needed. Second sense: a concomitant where conceptualizing both the concomitant and the thing together with the relation between them suffices for the mind to affirm the concomitance with certainty (jazm). An example of the second sense is also 'greater' for the whole (the same example works for both). The second sense is more general than the first because any concomitant that is clear in the first sense is also clear in the second (since if one conceptualization suffices, then two plus the relation also suffice), but not conversely. Some concomitants require more than mere conceptualization (e.g., an external proof). Therefore, the clear in the second sense includes all that are clear in the first sense plus others. The non-clear is the opposite: when conceptualization of the two terms and the relation does not yield certainty. Then the relationship of generality is reversed: the non-clear in the second sense (where the two conceptualizations are insufficient) is more specific than the non-clear in the first sense (where even the conceptualization of the alone is insufficient). Both types of non-clear exist in reality. The text ends by affirming that each type actually occurs.

Key Terms
اللازم
البين
الملزوم
التصور
الجزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The second sense is more general than the first.
C) Both are equally general.
D) The first sense does not exist in reality.

Key Takeaway
The clear concomitant has two meanings: a narrower one (conceptualization of the thing alone suffices) and a broader one (conceptualization of both terms and the relation yields certainty); the second meaning encompasses the first.

Distinguishing Self-Evident and Non-Self-Evident Implication
Classification of self-evident and non-self-evident implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an implication as self-evident (specific or general) or non-self-evident by testing whether mere conception of one or both terms suffices for certainty.

Core Idea
Implication's evidentness depends on whether mere conception suffices for certainty, not on needing a middle term.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum defines the crucial distinction between self-evident (bayyin) and non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin) necessary implication. It explains that self-evident implication can be known by merely conceiving one or both relata, while non-self-evident requires additional reasoning, intuition, or experience. Understanding this classification helps you evaluate when a logical connection is immediately obvious and when it requires proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the definitions of non-self-evident lazim in the more general and more specific senses. Note the examples: potency of writing for man (non-self-evident in the more general sense) and temporality of the world (non-self-evident in the more specific sense, needing the middle term 'change').
The more general sense requires something beyond conception (e.g., intuition), the more specific requires a middle term.
2
Extract the criterion
The single criterion for non-self-evident is insufficiency of mere conception of both extremes and the relation for certainty. This is not about needing a middle term; it may be intuition or experience.
Be careful: 'non-self-evident' does not automatically mean 'needs a middle term'.
3
Analyze the relationship between the two senses of self-evident
Every specific self-evident (sufficiency of one term) is general self-evident (sufficiency of both terms), but not vice versa. Understand why: if one term suffices, both terms also suffice, but the converse does not hold.
Al-Qutb's example: conception of malzum alone suffices; then conception of lazim with malzum also suffices, but not every case where two conceptions suffice does one suffice.
4
Apply the rule
Given a new example from Islamic theology (e.g., 'fire is hot'), test whether it is self-evident or non-self-evident. According to the passage, mere conception of fire and heat yields certainty for most people, so it is self-evident (possibly in the more general sense if both terms are needed).
This exercise reinforces the criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللازم الذي لا يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم، كالكتابة بالقوة للإنسان، ومقابل اللازم بالمعنى الأعم هو اللازم الذي لا يلزم من تصوره مع تصور الملزوم والنسبة بينهما الجزم باللزوم، كالحدوث للعالم ؛ فإنه لا يكفي لجزم العقل باللزوم بين الحدوث والعالم مجرد تصور الطرفين والنسبة بينهما، بل يحتاج إلى الوسط الدليل الذي هو التغير، فالحدوث بالنسبة إلى العالم غير بين. ولكن هذا لا يعني أن اللازم الغير البين هو المحتاج دائما إلى الوسط للجزم باللزوم ، وليس كذلك، بل لا يلزم من عدم افتقار اللزوم إلى وسط أنه يكفي فيه مجرد تصور اللازم والملزوم ؛ لجواز توقفه على شيء آخر، من حدس أو تجربة أو غير ذلك. والحاصل أن مدار كون اللازم غير بين عدم كفاية مجرد تصورِ الطرفين والنسبة بينهما في جزم العقل باللزوم، فافهم؛ فإنه دقيق. أما النسبة بَيْنَ البَيِّنِ بالمعنى الأخص والبَيِّن بالمعنى الأعم فواضح من تسميتهما ؛ فإن كل بين بالمعنى الأخص بَيِّنُ بالمعنى الأعم، وليس كل بين بالمعنى الأعم بينا بالمعنى الأخص ؛ لأن المعتبر في البين الأخص كفاية طرف واحد وهو الملزوم - في الجزم باللزوم، وقد توفر هذا في البين الأعم ؛ حيث فيه تصور طرفين والنسبة بينهما، فمتى يكفي تصور الملزوم في اللزوم يكفي تصور اللازم مع تصور الملزوم، وليس كلَّما يكفي التصورانِ يكفي تصور واحد على حد تعبير القطب في شرح الشمسية (٢٨١/١)، فإذا كان تصور طرف واحد كافيا في الجزم باللزوم فكيف لا يكون تصور طرفين ونسبة بينهما كافيا، وهذا واضح للمتأمل، فكل بين بالمعنى الأخص بين بالمعنى الأعم، وأما أن كل بين بالمعنى الأعم لا يلزم أن يكون بينا بالمعنى الأخص ؛ لأن البين الأعم لا يقتضي كفاية تصور طرفٍ واحد ـ هو الملزوم - في الجزم باللزوم، بل يتحقق البين الأعم بمجرد تصور طرف واحد ـ فيصدق عليه أنه بين بالمعنى الأخص أيضا كما يتحقق بتصور الطرفين والنسبة ، فلا يصدق بين بالمعنى الأخص، بل هو بين بالمعنى الأعم فقط. إذا عرفت أن البين بالمعنى أنه الثاني أعم من البين بالمعنى الأول ، وإليه أشار الدواني بقوله العموم إنما يظهر إذا اعتبر في الأول ما اعتبر في الثاني، وهو كفاية تصورهما الجزمَ باللزوم» عرفت النسبة بين نوعي الغير البين؛ فهي أن الغير البين بالمعنى الأعم أخص من الغير البين بالمعنى الأخص، يعني أن كل غير بين بالمعنى الأعم غير بين بالمعنى الأخص ، وليس كل غير بين بالمعنى الأخص غير بين بالمعنى الأعم ؛ لأنه كلما لا يكفي التصوران في الجزم باللزوم لا يكفي تصور الملزوم وحده، ولا ينعكس»، على حد تعبير المصنف في السعدية (١٥٨)؛ لأن القاعدة أن نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم، فلا حاجة للفطن إلى التطويل بذكر الأمثلة. فصل في الكليات الخمس بالضَّرُورَةِ) الأولية ، فلا يحتاج إلى تنبيه، فضلا عن دليل. (وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَازِمٌ) للملزوم، (وَإِلَّا) فيمكن انفكاكه فيمكن انفكاك اللازم ، وهذا (يَهْدِمُ أَصْلَ الْمُلَازَمَةِ) التي بين اللازم والملزوم، وهكذا لزوم اللزوم، (فَيَتَسَلْسَلُ اللُّزُومَاتُ . وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الاعْتِبَارِيَّةِ الِانْتِزَاعِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَيْسَ لَهَا تَحَقَّق في الذِّهْنِ بَعْدَ اعْتِبَارِهِ، فَيَنْقَطِعُ بِانْقِطَاعِ الاعتبار)، فلا تسلسل، وكان لمتوهم أن يتوهم أن الاعتبارية يوجب الاختراع فلم يكن واقعية، فأجاب بقوله : (نَعَمْ ، مَنْشَؤُهَا وَمَنْبَعُهَا مُتَحَقِّقُ) ، والاختراعي ما لا يكون موجودا بنفسه ولا بمنشئه، (وَ ذَلِكَ هُوَ)، أي وجود المنشأ (الْحَافِظُ لِنَفْس الْأَمْرِيَّةِ لِلاِنْتِزَاعِيَّاتِ ، مُتَنَاهِيَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ، مُرَتَّبَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُرَتَبَةِ) : فإِن معنى نفس الأمرية للانتزاعيات كون مناشيها في ذاتها من غير اعتبار المعتبر . بحيث يصح انتزاع تلك الانتزاعيات منها . فَقَوْلُهُمْ : التَّسَلْسُلُ فِيهَا أي في الانتزاعيات (لَيْسَ بِمُحَالٍ صَادِقٌ بِعَدَمِ الْمَوْضُوع) ، لا بتحققه وعدم ثبوت المحمول، (فَتَدَبَّرْ).
The necessary consequence (lazim) whose conception does not follow from the conception of the necessary cause (malzum), like the potency of writing for man. And the opposite of the lazim in the more general sense is the lazim such that from its conception together with the conception of the malzum and the relation between them there does not follow certainty of the implication, like temporality for the world; for mere conception of the two extremes and the relation between them is not sufficient for the mind's certainty of the implication between temporality and the world, but rather it needs the middle term, which is change. So temporality with respect to the world is non-self-evident. But this does not mean that the non-self-evident lazim always needs a middle term for certainty of implication; it is not so. Rather, it does not follow from the non-need of the implication for a middle term that mere conception of the lazim and the malzum suffices for it, because it may depend on something else, like intuition or experience or other. The upshot is that the criterion for the lazim being non-self-evident is the insufficiency of mere conception of the two extremes and the relation between them for the mind's certainty of the implication, so understand; for it is subtle. As for the relation between the self-evident in the more specific sense and the self-evident in the more general sense, it is clear from their naming; for every self-evident in the more specific sense is self-evident in the more general sense, but not every self-evident in the more general sense is self-evident in the more specific sense; because that which is considered in the more specific self-evident is the sufficiency of one extreme, namely the malzum, for certainty of implication, and this is present in the more general self-evident, since it involves conception of two extremes and the relation between them. So whenever conception of the malzum suffices for the implication, conception of the lazim together with conception of the malzum suffices, but not everything for which the two conceptions suffice does a single conception suffice, according to the expression of al-Qutb in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya (1/281). So if conception of one extreme is sufficient for certainty of implication, how could conception of two extremes and a relation between them not be sufficient? This is clear to the contemplator. So every self-evident in the more specific sense is self-evident in the more general sense, but it does not follow that every self-evident in the more general sense is self-evident in the more specific sense, because the more general self-evident does not require the sufficiency of a single extreme—namely the malzum—for certainty of implication; rather, the more general self-evident is realized by mere conception of one extreme—so it would also be true that it is self-evident in the more specific sense? But it is not true that it is self-evident in the more specific sense; rather, it is self-evident in the more general sense only. If you know that the self-evident in the second sense is more general than the self-evident in the first sense—and al-Dawani alluded to this by his saying, 'The generality only appears if that which is considered in the first is considered in the second, namely the sufficiency of their conception for certainty of implication'—you know the relation between the two types of non-self-evident: that the non-self-evident in the more general sense is more specific than the non-self-evident in the more specific sense, meaning that every non-self-evident in the more general sense is non-self-evident in the more specific sense, but not every non-self-evident in the more specific sense is non-self-evident in the more general sense; because whenever the two conceptions are insufficient for certainty of implication, the conception of the malzum alone is insufficient, and the converse does not hold, according to the expression of the author in al-Sa'diyya (158); because the rule is that the negation of the more specific is more general than the negation of the more general, so the intelligent person has no need for lengthy enumeration of examples. Chapter on the Five Universals: (by primary necessity), so it needs no reminder, let alone a proof. (And here there is a doubt, namely: that implication is necessary) for the malzum, (otherwise) it could be separated, so the lazim could be separated, and this (destroys the foundation of inseparability) that holds between the lazim and the malzum, and similarly the implication of implication, so implications regress indefinitely. (And its solution: that implication is among the intentional (i'tibariyya) abstracted (intiza'iyya) meanings that have no actuality in the mind after its consideration, so it is cut off by the cutting off of consideration), so no regress. And were someone to suppose that intentionality necessitates invention (ikhtira') and so it would not be real, he answered by saying: (Yes, their source and origin is realized), and the invented is that which does not exist by itself nor by its source. (And that is), i.e., the existence of the source, (the preserver of the reality-in-itself (nafs al-amriyya) for the abstracted entities, whether finite or infinite, ordered or unordered): for the meaning of reality-in-itself for abstracted entities is that their sources are in themselves without the consideration of the considerer, such that it is valid to extract those abstracted entities from them. So their saying: (regress in them) i.e., in the abstracted entities, (is not impossible) is true by the absence of the subject, not by its actuality and the non-establishment of the predicate, (so reflect).
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of necessary implication (luzum): self-evident (bayyin) and non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin). The author first defines the non-self-evident lazim as that whose conception does not follow simply from conceiving the malzum, giving the example of the potency of writing for humans. Then he discusses the more general sense of lazim, where even conceiving both relata and their relation is insufficient for certainty; here he gives the example of the world's temporality (huduth), which requires a middle term (change) to be known. He clarifies that non-self-evident implication does not always require a middle term; it may also depend on intuition or experience. The key is the insufficiency of mere conception. Next, he explains the relationship between the two senses of 'self-evident' (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass and al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm). Using terminology from al-Qutb, he shows that the specific sense is narrower: every specific self-evident is general, but not conversely. The specific sense requires only one extreme (the malzum) to be sufficient, while the general sense requires both extremes and the relation. He then analogously applies this to the non-self-evident types, concluding that the general non-self-evident is more specific (i.e., its extension is smaller) than the specific non-self-evident. Then follows a transitional heading 'Chapter on the Five Universals', but the content immediately addresses a skeptical objection: if the implication (luzum) itself is a lazim of the malzum, then it would require another implication, leading to infinite regress. The author resolves this by stating that implication is among the 'i'tibari intiza'i' meanings, which exist only by consideration and cease when consideration ceases. However, to avoid the charge of being purely invented, he clarifies that such meanings have a real source (mansha') that grounds their reality (nafs al-amr), preserving their objectivity. Therefore, regress in abstracted entities is not impossible in itself; rather, the statement that regress is impossible is true only because the subject (the regress) does not actually exist (since consideration stops).

Key Terms
اللازم
اللزوم
البين
المعاني الاعتبارية الانتنزاعية

Checkpoint

B) Every self-evident in the more specific sense is self-evident in the more general sense.

D) Neither implies the other.

Key Takeaway
The evidentness of an implication is determined by whether mere conception of the relata yields certainty, not by the need for a middle term.

Resolving the Infinite Regress of Implications
Solution to infinite regress of implications via i'tibari intiza'i meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why infinite regress of implications does not occur by analyzing implication as an intentional abstracted meaning with a real source.

Core Idea
Implication is a mind-dependent relation that ceases when consideration stops, but its foundation in reality preserves objectivity.

This passage addresses a skeptical objection: if the implication (luzum) itself is a lazim of the malzum, then it would require another implication, leading to an infinite chain. The solution involves understanding implication as an 'i'tibari intiza'i meaning—an intentional abstracted concept that exists only in the mind but has a real grounding. This insight prevents the regress while maintaining the reality of logical relations.

Guided Path
1
State the doubt
Read the doubt: implication (luzum) is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the malzum. If so, then that implication itself requires an implication that it is implied, leading to infinite regress. This would destroy the foundation of inseparability.
The doubt is a standard objection against the reality of relations.
2
Extract the solution
The author states that implication is from the 'i'tibariyya intiza'iyya meanings—intentional abstracted meanings that have no actual existence in the mind after consideration. Therefore, when consideration ceases, the implication ceases, stopping the regress.
This is an ontological solution: implication is not a real entity but a mental consideration.
3
Understand the nuance
To avoid the charge that this makes implication purely invented (ikhtira'), the author adds that these meanings have a real source (mansha') that grounds their 'nafs al-amr' (reality-in-itself). So the relation is not arbitrary but founded in external reality.
The distinction between i'tibari and ikhtira'i: i'tibari has a source, ikhtira'i does not.
4
Apply the solution to an Islamic example
In usul al-fiqh, the necessary link between an 'illa and a hukm is often considered a luzum. For instance, the prohibition of wine is necessarily implied by its intoxication. The implication itself is not an independent entity but an abstracted relation. If someone asked 'Why is that implication necessary?', the answer is that the implication is i'tibari, grounded in the real property of intoxication, and no regress follows.
This preserves the objectivity of qiyas while avoiding metaphysical regress.

(وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَازِمٌ) للملزوم، (وَإِلَّا) فيمكن انفكاكه فيمكن انفكاك اللازم ، وهذا (يَهْدِمُ أَصْلَ الْمُلَازَمَةِ) التي بين اللازم والملزوم، وهكذا لزوم اللزوم، (فَيَتَسَلْسَلُ اللُّزُومَاتُ . وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الاعْتِبَارِيَّةِ الِانْتِزَاعِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَيْسَ لَهَا تَحَقَّق في الذِّهْنِ بَعْدَ اعْتِبَارِهِ، فَيَنْقَطِعُ بِانْقِطَاعِ الاعتبار)، فلا تسلسل، وكان لمتوهم أن يتوهم أن الاعتبارية يوجب الاختراع فلم يكن واقعية، فأجاب بقوله : (نَعَمْ ، مَنْشَؤُهَا وَمَنْبَعُهَا مُتَحَقِّقُ) ، والاختراعي ما لا يكون موجودا بنفسه ولا بمنشئه، (وَ ذَلِكَ هُوَ)، أي وجود المنشأ (الْحَافِظُ لِنَفْس الْأَمْرِيَّةِ لِلاِنْتِزَاعِيَّاتِ ، مُتَنَاهِيَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ ، مُرَتَّبَةً أَوْ غَيْرَ مُرَتَبَةِ) : فإِن معنى نفس الأمرية للانتزاعيات كون مناشيها في ذاتها من غير اعتبار المعتبر . بحيث يصح انتزاع تلك الانتزاعيات منها . فَقَوْلُهُمْ : التَّسَلْسُلُ فِيهَا أي في الانتزاعيات (لَيْسَ بِمُحَالٍ صَادِقٌ بِعَدَمِ الْمَوْضُوع) ، لا بتحققه وعدم ثبوت المحمول، (فَتَدَبَّرْ).
The necessary consequence (lazim) whose conception does not follow from the conception of the necessary cause (malzum), like the potency of writing for man. And the opposite of the lazim in the more general sense is the lazim such that from its conception together with the conception of the malzum and the relation between them there does not follow certainty of the implication, like temporality for the world; for mere conception of the two extremes and the relation between them is not sufficient for the mind's certainty of the implication between temporality and the world, but rather it needs the middle term, which is change. So temporality with respect to the world is non-self-evident. But this does not mean that the non-self-evident lazim always needs a middle term for certainty of implication; it is not so. Rather, it does not follow from the non-need of the implication for a middle term that mere conception of the lazim and the malzum suffices for it, because it may depend on something else, like intuition or experience or other. The upshot is that the criterion for the lazim being non-self-evident is the insufficiency of mere conception of the two extremes and the relation between them for the mind's certainty of the implication, so understand; for it is subtle. As for the relation between the self-evident in the more specific sense and the self-evident in the more general sense, it is clear from their naming; for every self-evident in the more specific sense is self-evident in the more general sense, but not every self-evident in the more general sense is self-evident in the more specific sense; because that which is considered in the more specific self-evident is the sufficiency of one extreme, namely the malzum, for certainty of implication, and this is present in the more general self-evident, since it involves conception of two extremes and the relation between them. So whenever conception of the malzum suffices for the implication, conception of the lazim together with conception of the malzum suffices, but not everything for which the two conceptions suffice does a single conception suffice, according to the expression of al-Qutb in his commentary on al-Shamsiyya (1/281). So if conception of one extreme is sufficient for certainty of implication, how could conception of two extremes and a relation between them not be sufficient? This is clear to the contemplator. So every self-evident in the more specific sense is self-evident in the more general sense, but it does not follow that every self-evident in the more general sense is self-evident in the more specific sense, because the more general self-evident does not require the sufficiency of a single extreme—namely the malzum—for certainty of implication; rather, the more general self-evident is realized by mere conception of one extreme—so it would also be true that it is self-evident in the more specific sense? But it is not true that it is self-evident in the more specific sense; rather, it is self-evident in the more general sense only. If you know that the self-evident in the second sense is more general than the self-evident in the first sense—and al-Dawani alluded to this by his saying, 'The generality only appears if that which is considered in the first is considered in the second, namely the sufficiency of their conception for certainty of implication'—you know the relation between the two types of non-self-evident: that the non-self-evident in the more general sense is more specific than the non-self-evident in the more specific sense, meaning that every non-self-evident in the more general sense is non-self-evident in the more specific sense, but not every non-self-evident in the more specific sense is non-self-evident in the more general sense; because whenever the two conceptions are insufficient for certainty of implication, the conception of the malzum alone is insufficient, and the converse does not hold, according to the expression of the author in al-Sa'diyya (158); because the rule is that the negation of the more specific is more general than the negation of the more general, so the intelligent person has no need for lengthy enumeration of examples. Chapter on the Five Universals: (by primary necessity), so it needs no reminder, let alone a proof. (And here there is a doubt, namely: that implication is necessary) for the malzum, (otherwise) it could be separated, so the lazim could be separated, and this (destroys the foundation of inseparability) that holds between the lazim and the malzum, and similarly the implication of implication, so implications regress indefinitely. (And its solution: that implication is among the intentional (i'tibariyya) abstracted (intiza'iyya) meanings that have no actuality in the mind after its consideration, so it is cut off by the cutting off of consideration), so no regress. And were someone to suppose that intentionality necessitates invention (ikhtira') and so it would not be real, he answered by saying: (Yes, their source and origin is realized), and the invented is that which does not exist by itself nor by its source. (And that is), i.e., the existence of the source, (the preserver of the reality-in-itself (nafs al-amriyya) for the abstracted entities, whether finite or infinite, ordered or unordered): for the meaning of reality-in-itself for abstracted entities is that their sources are in themselves without the consideration of the considerer, such that it is valid to extract those abstracted entities from them. So their saying: (regress in them) i.e., in the abstracted entities, (is not impossible) is true by the absence of the subject, not by its actuality and the non-establishment of the predicate, (so reflect).
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of necessary implication (luzum): self-evident (bayyin) and non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin). The author first defines the non-self-evident lazim as that whose conception does not follow simply from conceiving the malzum, giving the example of the potency of writing for humans. Then he discusses the more general sense of lazim, where even conceiving both relata and their relation is insufficient for certainty; here he gives the example of the world's temporality (huduth), which requires a middle term (change) to be known. He clarifies that non-self-evident implication does not always require a middle term; it may also depend on intuition or experience. The key is the insufficiency of mere conception. Next, he explains the relationship between the two senses of 'self-evident' (al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass and al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm). Using terminology from al-Qutb, he shows that the specific sense is narrower: every specific self-evident is general, but not conversely. The specific sense requires only one extreme (the malzum) to be sufficient, while the general sense requires both extremes and the relation. He then analogously applies this to the non-self-evident types, concluding that the general non-self-evident is more specific (i.e., its extension is smaller) than the specific non-self-evident. Then follows a transitional heading 'Chapter on the Five Universals', but the content immediately addresses a skeptical objection: if the implication (luzum) itself is a lazim of the malzum, then it would require another implication, leading to infinite regress. The author resolves this by stating that implication is among the 'i'tibari intiza'i' meanings, which exist only by consideration and cease when consideration ceases. However, to avoid the charge of being purely invented, he clarifies that such meanings have a real source (mansha') that grounds their reality (nafs al-amr), preserving their objectivity. Therefore, regress in abstracted entities is not impossible in itself; rather, the statement that regress is impossible is true only because the subject (the regress) does not actually exist (since consideration stops).
